One Measurement Suffices: Seller's Measurement After Sale in Buyer's Presence
Ruling on measurement sufficiency after sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on measurement after sale and distinguish when a single measurement suffices versus when two are required.

Core Idea
Seller's single measurement after sale in buyer's presence suffices—no second measurement needed, except in salam with two transactions.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains a key principle in sales: measurement is part of delivery. Once the seller measures in the buyer's presence, the item is known and delivered. The commentary addresses an objection that two measurements are required and clarifies the exception for salam transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the seller measuring the goods after the sale contract. The ruling is that this measurement is sufficient (sahih); the buyer does not need to measure again.
The text states 'kafā bihi fī al-ṣaḥīḥ'—it suffices in the sound view.
2
Understand the scope of applicability
This ruling applies to goods sold by volume (kayl), weight (wazn), or count ('add). It does NOT apply to goods sold by length (dhar').
For length, no second measurement is needed because length is a description; any excess goes to the buyer.
3
Analyze the objection and its refutation
Some scholars argue that two measurements are required based on a hadith mentioning two sa'ahs. The Hanafi response limits that hadith to specific salam transactions where two sales combine.
The objection is refuted by showing that the hadith's context is exceptional.
4
Examine the salam exception
In a salam transaction where the buyer pays in advance and later receives goods, if two different transactions are combined (e.g., the seller buys from another and then delivers to the original buyer), two measurements may be required.
This is because the second transaction is like a new sale requiring its own measurement.
5
Determine the practical difference for length-measured items
For items sold by length, the seller's single measurement after the contract is not required to be done again. Any increase in length discovered later belongs to the buyer because the length is a specification.
This contrasts with volume/weight/count where measurement is essential for delivery.

Source Text

Likewise, if he measured it after the sale in the absence of the buyer, it suffices in the sound [view]: that is, if the seller measured after the sale in the presence of the buyer, this is sufficient, and it is not required that the buyer measure thereafter. The interpretation of the mentioned hadith is when the two transactions are combined with the condition of measurement, as will come in the chapter of Salam, which is when he pays in advance for a kurr of wheat, then when the term arrives, the person to whom salam was made buys a kurr from a man, or the owner of the salam ordered him to take possession for him, then takes possession for himself, so he measured it for him, then measured it for himself, it is permissible. [1] His saying: and likewise... etc.; that is, likewise, it is not considered if it was after the sale in the absence of the buyer, because measurement is part of delivery, and there is no delivery with absence. [2] His saying: in the sound [view]; a refutation of what was said: two measurements are required, the seller's measurement after the contract in the presence of the buyer, and the buyer's measurement before disposal, and the seller's measurement alone is not sufficient due to the apparent meaning of the hadith, for it considered two sa'ahs. [3] His saying: sufficient; because the sold item became known by a single measurement, and the meaning of delivery is realized, thus in al-Bahr. [5] His saying: and what is counted; other than dirhams and dinars, because disposal in them after possession before counting is permissible as they have stated. [6] His saying: not required... etc.; because the increase is for the buyer, for length measurement is a description in the garment. (al-Kifayah 6:141, al-Bahr al-Raiq 6:139).
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi ruling on the sufficiency of measurement after sale. The main text states that if the seller measures the goods after the sale in the presence of the buyer, it is sufficient and the buyer need not re-measure. This applies to goods sold by weight or count, but not to goods sold by length (like cloth). The commentary clarifies that measurement is part of delivery, and delivery requires the buyer's presence. The objection that two measurements are needed is refuted: one measurement by the seller in the buyer's presence suffices because the item becomes known and delivery is achieved. A special case of salam (advance payment) is mentioned where two measurements might be required due to two transactions combined. For counted items, dirhams and dinars are excepted because disposal after possession before counting is allowed. For length-measured items, no second measurement is required because the length is a description of the item, and the buyer benefits from any excess.

Key Terms
الكيل (al-kayl): Measurement by volume (e.g., for grains).
الذرع (al-dhar'): Measurement by length (e.g., for cloth).
التسليم (al-taslīm): Delivery; transferring possession of the sold item to the buyer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if the seller measures the goods after the sale in the presence of the buyer?
A) The sale is invalid because the buyer must measure.
B) The measurement suffices and the buyer need not re-measure.
C) Two measurements are required: one by seller and one by buyer.
D) The sale is suspended until the buyer measures.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, one measurement by the seller after the sale in the buyer's presence is sufficient for validity, except in salam where two transactions combine.

Disposal of the Price Before Receipt: Validity and Limitations
Ruling on validity of price disposal before receipt with limitation on increase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on disposing of the price before receipt and explain why increase is limited to the existence of the sold item.

Core Idea
The price can be disposed of before receipt because ownership removes the risk of rescission, but increase is conditional on the sold item's existence.

The Hanafi school permits a seller to dispose of the price (thaman) before actually receiving it from the buyer. This includes reducing the price, selling the debt for another item, or even increasing the price—but only while the sold item (muthman) still exists. The reasoning is that ownership (milk) grants full authority over the price, and the risk (gharar) that the sale might be rescinded is absent because the price is not physically tied to the sold item in a way that would cause uncertainty. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof, and the subtle condition on increases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
State the core ruling: disposal (tasarruf) of the price before receipt is valid. Distinguish between reduction (hatt) and increase (mazid). Reduction is always valid; increase is only valid while the sold item exists.
The ruling applies to both non-specific (currency) and specific (measured/weighed) items, with a caveat for per-unit pricing.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is rational (dalil aqli): ownership (milk) allows any disposition unless there is an impediment. The potential impediment here is gharar (risk of rescission if the sold item is destroyed), but that risk is absent because the price is a debt in the liability (dhimma) and not tied to a specific asset that could be destroyed.
For currency, the price is not specified by nature; for fungibles like grain, specification exists but the risk is still absent because the price is a debt.
3
Analyse the exception for specified per-unit items
If the price is per unit (e.g., per cubit of cloth) and the unit price is named, disposal before measurement is not allowed because the exact quantity is unknown. If the unit price is not named, disposal is valid before measurement.
This is an exception based on uncertainty (jahalah) in the price, not gharar of rescission.
4
Apply the reasoning to increase after destruction
Increase is only valid while the sold item exists because after destruction, the sale is no longer subject to increase—the transaction is effectively closed. The seller cannot later demand more for a destroyed item.
Reduction after destruction is valid because it is a voluntary concession by the seller, akin to forgiveness of debt.

Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage states a Hanafi ruling: it is valid to dispose of the price (thaman) before receiving it. The thaman is defined as a debt in the liability. The seller can take any substitute for the price, even a different item. Reduction (hatt) of the price is always valid. However, increasing the price (mazid) is only valid while the sold item (muthman) still exists; after its destruction, increase is invalid. The reason is that the seller's ownership allows disposal, and the risk (gharar) of the sale being rescinded due to destruction is absent because the price is not specified (in case of currency) or is considered not specified by specification. The commentary elaborates that this applies whether the price is a non-specific item (like currency) or a specific item (like measured goods). For items sold by length, if no price per unit is specified, disposal before measurement is allowed; if a per-unit price is specified, measurement must occur first. This ruling is based on the absence of gharar and the existence of ownership.

Key Terms
الثمن (al-thaman): The price that is a debt in the liability (dhimma) upon exchange; what the buyer owes.
الحط (al-hatt): Reduction of the price by the seller; valid unconditionally.
المزيد (al-mazid): Increase of the price by the seller; valid only while the sold item exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is it valid for the seller to increase the price after the sold item has been destroyed?
A) Yes, increase is always valid.
B) No, increase is only valid while the sold item exists.
C) Yes, increase is valid if the buyer agrees.
D) No, increase is never valid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, disposal of the price before receipt is valid; reduction is always valid, but increase is conditional on the sold item's existence.

Disposal of the Price Before Receipt: Permissibility and Rationale
Ruling with proof: permissibility of pre-receipt price disposal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on disposing of the price before physical receipt, identify the underlying rationale (istihsan based on absence of gharar al-infisakh), and distinguish between specific and non-specific prices.

Core Idea
Disposal of the price before receipt is valid because ownership is complete and the risk of rescission is absent when the price is not specifically determined.

In a sale, the price may become a debt in the seller's dhimmah before actual receipt. The Hanafi school, relying on istihsan, permits the seller to dispose of this debt (e.g., sell, gift, lease) before receiving it. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its rational proof, and an important exception when the price is specified per unit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is disposing of the price before physical receipt (qabd). The ruling: it is valid (sahih). The text states: 'وَصَحَّ التَّصَرُّفُ فِي الثَّمَنِ قَبْلَ قَبْضِهِ'.
Disposal includes sale, gift, lease, bequest, and any transfer of ownership.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is istihsan. The text says: 'لأنَّ المطلق للتصرُّف وهو الملك قائم، والمانع وهو غرر الانفساخ بالهلاك منتف; لعدم تعينها بالتعيين'. Ownership exists, and the impediment (risk of rescission if object destroyed) is absent because the price is not specifically designated.
The price is a debt (dayn) in the dhimmah, not a specific physical item.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The effective cause (illah) for permissibility is the absence of gharar al-infisakh. Since the price is a generic obligation, its destruction is not possible; only the object (mabi') can be destroyed. Thus, no risk of contract rescission affects the price.
Contrast with the object of sale where destruction can trigger rescission.
4
Note the exception
If the price is specified per unit (e.g., a price per cubit), then disposal is not allowed until measurement. The text cites al-Tibyan: 'هذا إذا لم يسم لكلِّ ذراع ثمناً ، وإن سمّى فلا يحل له التصرُّف فيه حتى يذرع'.
Specifying the price per unit makes it practically determined, reviving the gharar.
5
Apply the distinction
The ruling applies to both non-specific prices (like currency) and specific prices (like measured goods). The proof that the price is non-specific by designation applies to currency; for specific goods, the reasoning is broader. The text confirms: 'الدَّليلُ وهو انتفاء غرر الانفساخ بالهلاك ; لعدم تعينها بالتعيين'.
The proof is more specific than the claim, but the general permissibility stands.

لأنَّ المطلق للتصرُّف وهو الملك قائم، والمانع وهو غرر الانفساخ بالهلاك منتف; لعدم تعينها بالتعيين : أي في النقود، كما في «العناية» وغيرها. لكن المدعى عام، وهو التصرُّفُ في الثمن قبل القبض جائز مطلقاً، سواء كان مما لا يتعيّن أو مما يتعيّن كما مرّ ، والدَّليلُ وهو انتفاء غرر الانفساخ بالهلاك ; لعدم تعينها بالتعيين، فيكون الدليل أخص ، كذا في مجمع الأنهر. قوله : في الثمن ; الثمنُ ما يثبت في الذمة ديناً عند المقابلة، وهو والمثليات إذا كانت معينة وقوبلت بالأعيان ، أو غير معيَّنةٍ وصحبها حرف الباء ، وأما المبيع فهو القيميات والمثليات إذا قوبلت بنقد أو بعين، وهي غير معينة مثل : اشتريت كر بر بهذا العبد ، كذا في رد المحتار نقلاً عن «الشُّرُنْبُلاليّة». قوله : والحط عنه ...الخ ; أي وصح حط البائع عنه : أي عن الثمن، ولو بعد هلاك المبيع ; لأنه بحال يمكن إخراج البدل عمّا يقابله ; لكونه إسقاطاً، والإسقاط لا يستلزم ثبوت ما يقابله، فيثبت الحط في الحال، ويلتحق بأصل العقد استناداً. وصح المزيد : أي الزيادة فيه : أي في الثمن حال قيام المبيع إن قبل البائع في المجلس، حتى لو زاده فلم يقبل حتى تفرّقا بطلت الزيادة، كما في «الهداية» وغيرها. فعلى هذا لو قيد به لكان أولى; لأنه مما لا بدَّ منه لا بعد هلاك المبيع في ظاهرِ الرواية، إذ لو هلك المبيع أو تغير بتصرُّف المشتري فيه، حتى خرج عن إطلاق اسمه عليه كبر طحن أو خرج عن محلية البيع كعبد دبر ، لا تجوز الزيادة إذ ثبوتها ملحوظ في مقابلة الثَّمن ، وهو غير باق على حاله، فلم يتصوّر التقابل فيه، كذا في «مجمع الأنهر». وصورة الزيادة في الثمن أو الحط عنه، أن يشتري عيناً بمئةٍ ثم زاد عشرة مثلاً، أو باع عيناً بمئةٍ ثم زاد على المبيع شيئاً ، أو حط بعض الثمن، فكلُّ ذلك جائز، أما لو حط الكل فلا ; لأنه تبديل لأصل العقدِ لصيرورته هبة، لا تغيير لوصف العقد، فلا يلتحقُ. ثم: كتاب البيع / باب الإقالة والمرابحة والتولية وفي المبيع، ويتعلق استحقاقه بالجميع (وفي المبيع ) : أي صح الزيادة في المبيع، ولزم البائع دفعها إن قبل المشتري ذلك ; لأنه تصرف في حقه وملكه، ويلتحق بالعقد، فيصير حصة من الثمن، حتى لو هلكت الزيادة قبل القبض تسقط حصتها من الثمن، بخلاف الزيادة المتولدة من المبيع، حيث لا يسقط شيء بهلاكها قبل القبض. وكذا إذا زاد في الثمنِ عَرْضاً، كما لو اشتراهُ بمئة وتقابضا ثم زادَهُ المشتري عَرْضاً قيمته خمسون، وهلك العرضُ قبل التسليم ينفسخ العقد في ثلاثة، ولا يشترط للزيادة هاهنا قيام المبيع، فيصح بعد هلاكه بخلاف الزيادةِ في الثمن، كما في «البحر» وصرح به في «الكفاية» و«العناية».
And it is valid to dispose of the price before receiving it, and to reduce it and increase it while the object of sale is extant, not after its destruction. (It is valid to dispose of the price before receiving it) such as the seller taking from the buyer a garment in exchange for the price, and to reduce it and increase it while the object of sale is extant, not after its destruction. His statement: 'while the object of sale is extant' is connected to the increase, for increasing the price is not valid after the destruction of the object, but reducing it is valid. The possibility of deficiency only gives him an option, and he has dropped it by selling, unlike [a known] measure. And in al-Tibyan: This is if he did not specify a price for each cubit; but if he specified, then it is not permissible for him to dispose of it until he measures. His statement: 'and it is valid to dispose' of the price by sale, gift, lease, bequest, and making someone else owner of it, with or without compensation, before receiving it, whether it is non-specific like currency or specific like measured and weighed goods. Even if he sold camels for dirhams, or a sack of wheat, it is permissible for him to take something else in exchange for it because the basis for disposal—ownership—is present, and the impediment—the risk of rescission due to destruction—is absent due to their non-specificity by specification, i.e., in currency, as in al-Inayah and others. But the claim is general: disposal of the price before receipt is absolutely permissible, whether it is non-specific or specific as mentioned, and the proof is the absence of the risk of rescission due to destruction because of non-specificity by specification; thus the proof is more specific. This is from Majma' al-Anhur. His statement: 'in the price'; al-thaman is what is established in the dhimmah as a debt in exchange. It includes fungibles (mithliyat) if they are specific and exchanged for non-fungibles, or non-specific and accompanied by the letter ba. As for the object of sale (al-mabi'), it is the non-fungibles (qimiyat) and fungibles if exchanged for cash or a specific thing, and they are non-specific, like 'I bought a kor of wheat with this slave'. This is in Radd al-Muhtar quoting al-Sharnubalaliyya. His statement: 'and reducing it... etc.'; meaning: it is valid for the seller to reduce it, i.e., the price, even after the destruction of the object of sale, because it is possible to extract compensation for what it corresponds to, since it is a cancellation, and cancellation does not necessitate establishing its counterpart. So the reduction is established immediately and attaches to the original contract retroactively. And the increased (al-mazid) is valid: i.e., the increase in it, i.e., in the price, while the object of sale is extant, provided the seller accepts it in the session. Even if he increased it and he did not accept until they separated, the increase is void, as in al-Hidayah and others. Thus, if it were restricted by that, it would be better because it is indispensable; not after the destruction of the object of sale in the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah). So if the object of sale is destroyed or altered by the buyer's disposition such that it leaves the absolute name of the object, like wheat ground, or leaves the locus of the sale, like a slave who is made a mudabbar, the increase is not permissible because its establishment is considered in exchange for the price, and it is not remaining in its state, so exchange is not conceivable therein. This is from Majma' al-Anhur. And the form of increase in the price or reduction thereof is that he buys a specific thing for one hundred, then increases ten, for example, or sells a specific thing for one hundred, then adds something to the object of sale, or reduces part of the price; all of that is permissible. But if he reduces the whole, it is not, because it is a substitution of the original contract, making it a gift, not a change in the description of the contract, so it does not attach. Then: The Book of Sales / Chapter of Rescission, Murabahah, and Tawliyah: And in the object of sale, and its entitlement attaches to all. (And in the object of sale): i.e., it is valid to increase in the object of sale, and the seller must deliver it if the buyer accepts it, because it is a disposition in his right and property, and it attaches to the contract, so it becomes a portion of the price. Even if the increase is destroyed before receipt, its portion of the price falls, unlike the increase that is generated from the object of sale, where nothing falls due to its destruction before receipt. Likewise, if he increases the price with a commodity, as if he bought it for one hundred and they took possession, then the buyer added a commodity worth fifty, and the commodity was destroyed before delivery, the contract is rescinded in three? And here the existence of the object of sale is not a condition for the increase, so it is valid after its destruction, unlike the increase in the price, as in al-Bahr and stated in al-Kifayah and al-Inayah.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on a ruling from al-Wiqayah regarding the permissibility of disposing of the price (thaman) before physical receipt (qabd) in a sale contract. The ruling states that such disposition—whether it be sale, gift, lease, or any other transfer—is valid, and likewise reduction (hatt) of the price or increase (ziyada) in it is permissible, but with a crucial condition: the increase is only valid while the object of sale (mabi') is still extant, whereas reduction is valid even after the object's destruction.

The commentary begins by clarifying the ruling for the price: the seller may, before actually receiving the price, use it in exchange for something else, because ownership of the price is already established in his dhimmah (as a debt). The impediment that normally prevents such disposal—the risk that the contract might be rescinded if the object perishes (gharar al-infisakh)—is absent here because the price is not specifically identified in the sense that it is a generic obligation. The proof is rational: the seller has complete ownership, and the only potential obstacle (the risk of cancellation) is removed since the price is not tied to a specific item that could be destroyed. This applies whether the price is non-specific (like currency) or specific (like measured goods). However, there is a note from al-Tibyan: if each unit (e.g., per cubit) has a named price, then disposal is not allowed until actual measurement, indicating that specificity revives the risk.

The commentator then defines key terms: 'price' (thaman) is what becomes a debt in the dhimmah in exchange for the object; 'object of sale' (mabi') is the non-generic items (qimiyat) or generic items (mithliyat) when exchanged for cash or a specific countervalue. The reduction (hatt) of the price is valid even after the object's destruction because it is an act of cancellation (isqat) which does not require a continuing countervalue; it attaches retroactively to the original contract. By contrast, an increase in the price is valid only while the object exists and if the seller accepts it in the same session; if they separate before acceptance, the increase is void. If the object is destroyed or fundamentally altered (e.g., wheat ground into flour) so that it no longer bears the same name, the increase is impermissible because the intended correspondence (taqabul) between price and object is lost.

The passage then turns to the object of sale. Increasing the object itself (e.g., giving an extra item) is valid; the seller must deliver it if the buyer accepts. This increase becomes part of the price, so if it perishes before receipt, its share of the price falls. This contrasts with natural increase (e.g., growth) from the object, which is not affected. Finally, an increase in the price using a commodity (as opposed to cash) is treated differently: here the existence of the object is not required, so the increase can occur even after the object's destruction. These distinctions illustrate the Hanafi reliance on istihsan and the nuanced treatment of gharar.

Throughout, the commentary follows the original matn and supplements it with authoritative references (al-Tibyan, al-Inayah, Majma' al-Anhur, al-Hidayah, etc.), making clear that the relied-upon position is from the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
الثَّمن (al-thaman): The price that becomes a debt in the dhimmah in exchange for the object of sale (mabi'). It is the countervalue in a sale contract.
المبيع (al-mabi'): The object of sale; it can be non-fungibles (qimiyat) or fungibles (mithliyat) when exchanged for cash or a specific countervalue.
غرر الانفساخ (gharar al-infisakh): The risk that the contract might be rescinded (infisakh) due to the destruction of the subject matter, which is a potential impediment to disposition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the validity of disposing of the price before receipt?
A) The seller has the buyer's permission
B) Ownership is present and the risk of rescission is absent
C) The price is always currency
D) The object of sale must be in existence

Key Takeaway
Disposal of the price before receipt is valid in Hanafi fiqh because the seller owns the debt and there is no gharar al-infisakh when the price is a non-specific obligation.

Conditions for Valid Increase or Reduction in a Sale Contract
Explains validity conditions for increase or reduction in sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid increase or reduction in price or sold commodity after a sale contract, and apply them to scenarios involving destruction or change of the subject matter.

Core Idea
An increase or reduction in a sale is valid only if the sold commodity still exists and both parties accept in the same session.

This lesson expands on a key principle of Hanafi sales law: after a contract is concluded, the parties may agree to increase the price, add to the sold commodity, or deduct part of the price. The passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya explains that such modifications are treated as retroactive annexations to the original contract, but they are subject to strict conditions. We will walk through the conditions, the rationale, and the exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and its default ruling
Determine what legal act the passage discusses: increasing the price or the sold commodity, or reducing the price. Confirm that the default ruling is ṣaḥīḥ (valid) if conditions are met.
The passage affirms jawaaz (permissibility) as the default.
2
Isolate the conditions for validity
List the conditions mentioned: (a) the sold commodity must exist (for a price increase), (b) acceptance must occur in the same session before the parties separate, and (c) the increase or reduction must be a partial change, not a full conversion to a gift.
A full reduction of price turns the sale into a gift (hibah) and is invalid as an annexation.
3
Apply the condition to destruction scenarios
Consider what happens if the sold commodity perishes or is fundamentally changed before acceptance of the increase. The passage gives examples: wheat ground into flour, a slave made mudabbar. In these cases, the increase is invalid.
The rationale is that the price is no longer in its original state, so mutual exchange becomes inconceivable.
4
Distinguish between partial and full reduction
Explain why a partial reduction (ḥaṭṭ) is valid and annexed to the original contract, but a full reduction is not. The full reduction replaces the contract with a gift, changing the essence of the transaction.
This is based on the principle that dropping (isqāt) does not require a substitute.
5
Analyze the special case of increase with a commodity
The footnote introduces a scenario where the buyer increases the price with a commodity (e.g., goods worth fifty) after receipt. Here, the increase is valid even if the sold commodity perishes, unlike a cash increase.
This exception shows that the ruling is not absolute; context matters.

كتاب البيع / باب الإقالة والمرابحة والتولية
(وفي المبيع) : أي صح الزيادة في المبيع، ويتعلق استحقاقه بالجميع هلاك المبيع؛ لأنه بحال يمكن إخراج البدل عمّا يقابله؛ لكونه إسقاطاً، والإسقاط لا يستلزم ثبوت ما يقابله، فيثبت الحط في الحال، ويلتحق بأصل العقد استناداً. وصح المزيد: أي الزيادة فيه: أي في الثمن حال قيام المبيع إن قبل البائع في المجلس، حتى لو زاده فلم يقبل حتى تفرّقا بطلت الزيادة، كما في «الهداية» وغيرها. فعلى هذا لو قيد به لكان أولى؛ لأنه مما لا بدَّ منه لا بعد هلاك المبيع في ظاهر الرواية، إذ لو هلك المبيع أو تغير بتصرف المشتري فيه، حتى خرج عن إطلاق اسمه عليه كبر طحن أو خرج عن محلية البيع كعبد دبر، لا تجوز الزيادة إذ ثبوتها ملحوظ في مقابلة الثمن، وهو غير باق على حاله، فلم يتصور التقابل فيه، كذا في «مجمع الأنهر». وصورة الزيادة في الثمن أو الحط عنه، أن يشتري عيناً بمئة ثم زاد عشرة مثلاً، أو باع عيناً بمئة ثم زاد على المبيع شيئاً، أو حط بعض الثمن، فكل ذلك جائز، أما لو حط الكل فلا؛ لأنه تبديل لأصل العقد لصيرورته هبة، لا تغيير لوصف العقد، فلا يلتحق.
[1] قوله: وفي المبيع؛ أي وكذا صح الزيادة في المبيع، ولزم البائع دفعها إن قبل المشتري ذلك؛ لأنه تصرف في حقه وملكه، ويلتحق بالعقد، فيصير حصة من الثمن، حتى لو هلكت الزيادة قبل القبض تسقط حصتها من الثمن، بخلاف الزيادة المتولدة من المبيع، حيث لا يسقط شيء بهلاكها قبل القبض.
وكذا إذا زاد في الثمن عرضاً، كما لو اشتراه بمئة وتقابضا ثم زاده المشتري عرضاً قيمته خمسون، وهلك العرض قبل التسليم ينفسخ العقد في ثلاثة، ولا يشترط للزيادة هاهنا قيام المبيع، فيصح بعد هلاكه بخلاف الزيادة في الثمن، كما في «البحر» وصرح به في «الكفاية» و«العناية».
The Book of Sale / Chapter of Iqaalah, Muraabahah, and Tawliyah. (And in the sold commodity) means: the increase in the sold commodity is valid, and its entitlement is attached to the whole destruction of the sold commodity; because it is in a state where the substitute can be extracted against its counterpart; because it is a dropping, and dropping does not necessitate the establishment of its counterpart, so the reduction is established immediately and is annexed to the original contract retrospectively. And the increased [amount] is valid: meaning the increase in it, i.e. in the price, while the sold commodity exists, if the seller accepts it in the session. So if he increases it and he does not accept until they separate, the increase becomes void, as in al-Hidayah and others. So upon this, if it were restricted by it, it would be better; because it is indispensable. Not after the destruction of the sold commodity in the apparent transmission, for if the sold commodity is destroyed or changed by the buyer's disposition such that it departs from the absolute application of its name, like wheat being ground, or departs from being fit for sale, like a slave who is made mudabbar, the increase is not permissible, because its establishment is considered in exchange for the price, and it is not remaining in its state, so the mutual exchange is not conceivable, as in Majma‘ al-Anhur. And the form of increase in the price or reduction from it is that one buys an item for one hundred, then he increases ten, for example; or he sells an item for one hundred, then he increases something extra to the sold commodity; or he deducts part of the price; all of that is permissible. But if he deducts the whole, then no; because it is a replacement of the original contract, making it a gift, not a change of the description of the contract, so it is not annexed. [1] His statement: "And in the sold commodity" means: and likewise the increase in the sold commodity is valid, and it is incumbent on the seller to deliver it if the buyer accepts it; because it is a disposition in his right and ownership, and it is annexed to the contract, so it becomes a portion of the price. So if the increase perishes before delivery, its portion of the price falls, contrary to the increase generated from the sold commodity, where nothing falls due to its perishing before delivery. And likewise, if he increases in the price with a commodity, like if he buys it for one hundred and they receive payment, then the buyer increases him with a commodity worth fifty, and the commodity perishes before delivery, the contract is rescinded in three [parts]. And it is not required here for the increase that the sold commodity exist, so it is valid after its destruction, contrary to the increase in the price, as in al-Bahr and affirmed in al-Kifayah and al-‘Inayah.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage from the commentary 'عمدة الرعاية' expands on the ruling of increase (زيادة) and reduction (حط) in sales contracts. The author first explains that an increase in the sold commodity or the price is valid as long as the subject matter exists and the parties accept in the session. If they separate before acceptance, the increase becomes void. This is the ruling in ظاهر الرواية (the apparent transmission of Imam Muhammad). The key principle is that the increase is considered a retroactive annexation to the original contract, not a new contract. However, if the sold commodity perishes or is fundamentally changed (e.g., wheat ground into flour, or a slave made mudabbar), the increase is not valid because the price is no longer in its original state, so mutual exchange is impossible. The passage then gives practical examples: increasing the price by ten, adding an extra item to the sale, or deducting part of the price—all are permissible. But deducting the entire price is not permissible because that turns the sale into a gift, which changes the very nature of the contract. The footnote [1] clarifies that if the increase itself perishes before delivery, its portion of the price is deducted, unlike the case of natural growth from the sold item (like fruit from a tree), which does not affect the price. Finally, there is a special case: if the buyer increases the price with a commodity (عرض) after receipt, and that commodity perishes before delivery, the original contract is rescinded in three-thirds (i.e., the increase is considered a separate contract). In this case, the existence of the sold commodity is not required for the increase to be valid, unlike the increase in cash price.

Key Terms
الإقالة (al-iqaalah): The rescission of a sale by mutual consent.
المرابحة (al-muraabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost and sells at a profit margin.
الزيادة (al-ziyadah): An increase in the price or in the sold commodity agreed upon after the contract.
الحط (al-hatt): A reduction or deduction from the price agreed upon after the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if the seller increases the price after the contract but the buyer does not accept until after they separate?
A) The increase is valid and binding.
B) The increase becomes void.
C) The increase is valid only if the sale object has been delivered.
D) The increase is valid but requires a new contract.

Key Takeaway
An increase or reduction in a sale contract is valid only if the sold commodity exists at the time and the other party accepts in the same session.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Increase After Destruction of Sale Object
Intra-Hanafi dispute on increase after destruction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on the validity of increasing the price or sale object after the destruction of the sale object, identify the mufta bihi position, and apply the ruling to practical scenarios.

Core Idea
Increase after destruction changes the contract description; thus it is invalid in ظاهر الرواية despite a weak transmission allowing it.

In Hanafi sales law, an agreement to increase the price or the sale object after the contract is generally treated as an annexation to the original contract. However, when the sale object is destroyed before the increase, a critical dispute arises: does the increase take effect? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri‘ayah presents a transmission from al-Kafi that permits it, while the well-known zahir al-riwayah declares it invalid. Understanding this dispute is essential for correctly applying Hanafi law in cases where goods are lost before a modification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
The passage reports that in al-Kafi, increase after destruction is valid in one transmission but invalid in the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah).
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not a cross-madhhab one.
2
Understand the reasoning
The increase changes the contract's description, requiring the contract to be existent; destruction of the sale object ends the contract basis.
The reasoning is textual: 'because the increase changes the contract from one description to another, so it requires the existence of the contract, and its existence depends on the existence of the sold commodity.'
3
Note the unresolved contradiction
The author states that there is a contradiction between what is reported in al-Kafi and other works like al-Kifayah and al-‘Inayah, and calls for reconciliation.
This indicates that later scholars were aware of the discrepancy but did not provide a definitive resolution in this text.
4
Explore practical consequences
The commentary then details effects on seller's right to detain, buyer's obligation, third-party claims, and pre-emption rights, all based on the ruling that increase after destruction is invalid.
These consequences assume the mufta bihi position of invalidity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية
يمكن أن يراد به أن البائع يكون مستحقاً بجميع الثمن من الزائد والمزيد عليه، والمشتري يستحق جميع المبيع من الزائد والمزيد عليه، ويمكن أن يراد أنه إذا استحق مستحق المبيع أو الثمن، فالاستحقاق يتعلق بجميع ما يقابله من الزائد والمزيد عليه، فلا يكون الزائد صلة مبتدأة.
وقال يعقوب باشا في «حاشية» هذا الكتاب: وهاهنا كلام وهو أن الظاهر من «الكافي» أن الزيادة بعد تلف المبيع سواء أكانت في الثمن أو في المبيع تصح في رواية، ولا تصح في ظاهر الرواية؛ لأن الزيادة تغير العقد من وصف إلى وصف، فتستدعي قيام العقد، وقيامه بقيام المبيع. وذكر في بعض شروح «الجامع الصغير»: إن الزيادة في المبيع أو الثمن إنما تجوز إذا كان المبيع قائماً، ولا تجوز لو كان هالكاً، فبين هذا وبين ما ذكر في «الكفاية» و«العناية» منافاة، فليتأمل في التوفيق. انتهى.
[1] قوله: إن البائع... الخ؛ فإن للبائع أن يحبس المبيع ما لم يستوف المزيد والمزيد عليه من الثمن إذا كان الثمن حالاً، وليس للمشتري أن يمنع الزيادة بعد ذلك؛ لأنها استحقت بأصل العقد، وكذلك المشتري ليس له مطالبة البائع بتسليم المبيع ما لم يسم المزيد والمزيد عليه من الثمن. وكذلك المشتري يرجع على البائع بجميع ذلك أعني بالأصل والزيادة إذا استحق المبيع، وفي صورة الحط للمشتري مطالبة البائع بتسليم المبيع إذا سلم ما بقي بعد الحط من الثمن، وكذلك الشفيع يستحق المبيع بما بقي بعد الحط، كذا صرح به العيني.
[2] قوله: ويمكن... الخ؛ اعترض عليه صاحب «الدرر» بأنه لا يمكن ذلك؛ لأن مدار هذا الاستحقاق على الدعوى والبيئة، فإن ادعى المستحق مجرد المزيد عليه وأثبته أخذه، وإن ادعاه مع الزيادة وأثبته أخذه، وكذا إن ادعى الزيادة فقط.
Zubdat al-Nihayah li-‘Umdat al-Ri‘ayah. It can be meant that the seller is entitled to the entire price, both the increased amount and that upon which the increase was made, and the buyer is entitled to all of the sold commodity, both the increased part and that upon which the increase was made. And it can be meant that if a claimant proves entitlement to the sold commodity or the price, the entitlement pertains to all that corresponds to it of the increased amount and that upon which the increase was made, so the increase is not an independent grant. And Ya‘qub Pasha said in his gloss on this book: Here there is a discussion: The apparent [teaching] from al-Kafi is that increase after the loss of the sold commodity, whether in the price or in the sold commodity, is valid according to one transmission, but is not valid in the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah); because the increase changes the contract from one description to another, so it requires the existence of the contract, and its existence depends on the existence of the sold commodity. And it is mentioned in some commentaries of al-Jami‘ al-Saghir that increase in the sold commodity or the price is only permissible if the sold commodity exists, and not permissible if it is destroyed. So there is contradiction between this and what is mentioned in al-Kifayah and al-‘Inayah, so one must reflect on reconciliation. End. [1] His statement: 'Indeed, the seller...' to the end: The seller has the right to retain the sold commodity until he receives the increased amount and that upon which the increase was made from the price, if the price is payable immediately. And it is not for the buyer to prevent the increase after that, because it is entitled by the original contract. Likewise, the buyer does not have the right to demand delivery of the sold commodity until he pays the increased amount and that upon which the increase was made from the price. And likewise, the buyer has recourse against the seller for all of that—namely, the original and the increase—if the sold commodity is [later] claimed by a third party. And in the case of reduction, the buyer has the right to demand delivery of the sold commodity if he pays what remains after the reduction of the price. And likewise, the pre-emptor (shafi‘) is entitled to the sold commodity for what remains after the reduction, as stated by al-‘Ayni. [2] His statement: 'And it can be...' to the end: The author of al-Durar objected to this, saying that it is not possible because the basis of this entitlement is on claim and evidence. If the claimant claims only the item upon which the increase was made and proves it, he takes it; if he claims it along with the increase and proves it, he takes it; and likewise if he claims only the increase.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage from the commentary addresses the interpretation of the phrase 'وفي المبيع' and presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute. The first paragraph gives two possible meanings: either the seller and buyer are entitled to both the original and the increased part, or that if a third party proves entitlement to the sale object, the entitlement covers both the original and the increase, meaning the increase is not an independent grant. The core issue is the validity of increase after the destruction of the sale object. Ya‘qub Pasha reports that in al-Kafi, there is a transmission that allows increase even if the sale object perishes, but this contradicts the well-known ظاهر الرواية (the apparent transmission of Imam Muhammad), which denies its validity. The reasoning is that increase changes the contract's description and requires the contract's existence, which depends on the sale object's existence. This dispute remains unresolved ('must reflect on reconciliation'). The commentary then discusses the practical consequences: the seller can detain the sale until full payment (including increase), and the buyer cannot refuse the increase since it is annexed to the contract. In case of a third party claiming ownership, the buyer can recover both the original price and the increase from the seller. For reduction (hatt), the buyer must pay the reduced amount. The pre-emptor (shafi‘) also takes the sale object for the reduced amount. Finally, an objection from the author of al-Durar: the entitlement depends on the claimant's claim and evidence; he can claim just the original, or with increase, or just the increase—it is not necessarily that the increase is annexed automatically.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The apparent or well-known transmission from the Hanafi imams, especially from the six books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.
الكافي (al-Kafi): A Hanafi fiqh work by al-Hakim al-Shahid al-Marwazi, summarizing the zahir al-riwayah.
الشفيع (al-shafi‘): The pre-emptor who has the right of pre-emption (shuf‘ah) in property sales.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does Ya‘qub Pasha report about the ruling on increase after the destruction of the sale object?
A) It is invalid in all transmissions.
B) It is valid in the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah) but invalid in some other transmissions.
C) It is valid in one transmission but invalid in the apparent transmission.
D) It is always valid regardless of the condition of the sale object.

Key Takeaway
Increase after destruction of the sale object is invalid in the authoritative Hanafi position (zahir al-riwayah), as it changes the contract description after its subject matter has ceased to exist.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Hanafi Position on Price Increase (Ziyada) and Decrease (Hatt) After Sale
Explaining the Hanafi view on ziyada and hatt attaching to the original contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ziyada and hatt, explain the Hanafi proof based on iqala, and apply the ruling to muraabaha and shuf'a calculations.

Core Idea
Price adjustments after sale attach to the original contract, not as new gifts.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi ruling: when a buyer and seller agree to increase or decrease the price after a sale, does this create a new contract or modify the original? The Hanafi school, unlike Zufar and al-Shafi'i, holds that such adjustments attach to the original contract. The evidence rests on the parties' ability to annul the contract entirely (iqala), making it easier to change its description. This ruling has practical implications for muraabaha, tawliya, and shuf'a.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Ziyada and hatt are valid and attach to the original contract; they modify the description (e.g., from loss to profit) but do not create a new contract.
This is the position of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) and al-Shafi'i hold that ziyada and hatt do not attach; they are new gifts (ibtida' sila) independent of the original contract.
They argue that the buyer already owns the item, so paying more for it is invalid.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Opponent: The buyer owns the sold item by the original contract; increasing price means paying for one's own property. Reducing leaves part of the item without consideration. Therefore, adjustments cannot be part of the original contract.
This is a logical objection based on the concept of ownership.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafis respond: The parties can change the contract from one lawful description to another (e.g., profitable to loss-making). Since they have authority to annul the whole contract (iqala), they certainly have authority to change its description. Changing a description is easier than changing the essence. Analogy: stipulating an option after the contract also changes its attribute.
The key principle: تصرف في الوصف أهون من التصرف في الأصل.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: In tawliya (sale at cost) and muraabaha (sale at profit), the Hanafis calculate based on the adjusted price (including increase or after reduction). The pre-emptor (shafi') takes at the reduced price. Zufar/Shafi'i treat adjustments as gifts, so they do not affect these calculations.
This highlights the real-world impact of the dispute.

Source Text

العقد من وصف إلى وصف، فتستدعي قيام العقد، وقيامه بقيام المبيع. وذكر في بعض شروح «الجامع الصغير»: إنَّ الزيادة في المبيع أو الثمن إنما تجوز إذا كان المبيع قائماً ، ولا تجوز لو كان هالكاً ، فبين هذا ؛ اعترض عليه صاحب «الدرر» بأنه لا يمكن ذلك ؛ لأنَّ مدار هذا الاستحقاق على الدعوى والبيئة، فإن ادعى المستحق مجرد المزيد عليه وأثبته أخذه ، وإن ادَّعاه مع الزيادةِ وأثبته أخذه ، وكذا إن ادعى الزيادة فقط. قوله : كما هو مذهب زفر والشافعي ؛ فإنَّ عندهما لا يصح الحط والمزيد، على اعتبار الالتحاق بأصل العقد، بل على اعتبار ابتداء الصلة ؛ لأنه لا يمكن تصحيحُ الزيادة ثمناً؛ لأنه يصير ملكه عوض ملكه ، فإنَّ المشتري ملك المبيع بالعقد بالمسمّى من الثمن قبل الزيادة. فلي زاد في الثمن مع ذلك، كانت الزيادة لأجل ملك نفسه، وهو المبيع، وذلك لا يجوز، فلا يلتحق الزيادة بأصل العقد، وكذلك الحطّ ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ الثمن صارَ مقابلاً بكل المبيع، فلا يمكن إخراجُ بعضه فلو أخرج بقي المبيعُ بلا عوض، وذا لا يجوز، ولا يمكن أن يجعل الباقي في مقابلة الكلّ ؛ لانتفاء العقد الجديد، فصار بُرا مبتدأ. ولنا : إنَّ البائع والمشتري بالخط والزيادة يغيران العقـد مـن وصف مشروع إلى وصف مشروع آخر، وهو كونه رابحاً أو خاسراً أو عدلاً، فإنَّ البيع شرع خاسراً ورابحاً وعادلاً، والزيادة في الثمن تجعل الخاسر عادلاً، والعادل رابحاً، والحط يجعل الرابح عادلاً، والعادل خاسراً. وللعاقدين ولاية رفع العقدِ بالإقالة محاولاً أن يكون لهما ولاية التغير من وصف إلى وصف ؛ لأنَّ التصرف في وصف الشيء أهون من التصرف في أصله، فالذي يملك التصرُّف في الأصل أولى بأن يملك التصرُّف في الوصف، وصار كما إذا أسقط الخيار أو شرطاه بعد العقد ، بأن عقدا عقداً ثمَّ شرطا الخيار بعده ، فقد يغير العقد من صفة اللزوم إلى غيره. ثم إذا صح الزيادة والحط فهما يلتحقان بأصل العقد ؛ لأنَّ وصف الشيء يقوم به لا بنفسه، وفائدة الالتحاق تظهر في التولية والمرابحة والشفعة والاستحقاق وحبس المبيع وفسادِ الصرف فيولي ويرابحُ على الكلِّ في الزيادة، وعلى الباقي بعد المحطوط، ويأخذ الشفيع بما بقي في الحط. وإنما كان له أن يأخذ بدون الزيادة لما فيها من إبطال حقه الثابت، فلا يملكانه ، ويرجع المشتري على البائع بالكل، ولو أجاز المستحق البيع أخذ الكل، وله حبسه حتى يقبض الزيادة، ولو باع الدراهم بالدراهم متساوية ، ثمَّ زادَ أحدهما أو حط وقبل
Ya'qub Pasha said in his gloss on this book: Here there is discussion, namely that the apparent from al-Kafi is that the increase after the destruction of the sold item, whether it is in the price or in the sold item, is valid in one narration, and invalid in the apparent narration; because the increase changes the contract from one description to another, so it requires the continuation of the contract, and its continuation is by the continuation of the sold item. And it is mentioned in some commentaries of al-Jami' al-Saghir: Indeed the increase in the sold item or the price is only permitted if the sold item is extant, and not permitted if it is destroyed. So between this and what is mentioned in al-Kifaya and al-Inaya there is contradiction, so one should reflect on reconciliation. End. His saying: Indeed the seller... etc.; For the seller may detain the sold item as long as he has not received the additional amount and the original amount of the price, if the price is prompt, and the buyer may not prevent the increase after that, because it is owed by the original contract. And likewise, the buyer may not demand the seller to deliver the sold item unless he pays the additional amount and the original amount of the price. And likewise, the buyer has recourse against the seller for all of that, meaning the original and the increase, if the sold item is reclaimed. And in the case of reduction, the buyer may demand the seller to deliver the sold item if he pays what remains after the reduction of the price. And likewise, the pre-emptor is entitled to the sold item at what remains after the reduction. Thus al-'Ayni explicitly stated it. His saying: And it is possible... etc.; The author of al-Durar objected to it, saying that this is not possible, because the basis of this entitlement is on claim and evidence. If the claimant claims merely the original amount and proves it, he takes it; if he claims it along with the increase and proves it, he takes it; and likewise if he claims only the increase. His saying: As is the madhhab of Zufar and al-Shafi'i; For according to them, reduction and increase are not valid on the basis of attaching to the original contract, but rather on the basis of a new gift; because it is not possible to validate the increase as price, because then he would become owner of a substitute for his own property. The buyer owned the sold item by the contract at the named price before the increase. If he increases the price despite that, the increase is for his own property, which is the sold item, and that is not permissible. So the increase does not attach to the original contract. Likewise, reduction, because all of the price became in exchange for all of the sold item; it is not possible to remove some of it. If he removes some, the sold item remains without compensation, and that is not permissible. And it is not possible to make the remaining price in exchange for the whole, due to the absence of a new contract. So it becomes a new gift. Our evidence: The seller and buyer, through reduction and increase, change the contract from one lawful description to another lawful description, i.e., being profitable or loss-making or just. For sale is legislated as loss-making, profitable, and just. Increasing the price makes the loss-making transaction just, and the just transaction profitable. Reduction makes the profitable transaction just, and the just transaction loss-making. And the contracting parties have authority to rescind the contract by iqala, so it is more appropriate that they have authority to change it from one description to another, because dealing with the description of a thing is easier than dealing with its essence. The one who has authority over the essence is more entitled to have authority over the description. It is like when they drop an option or stipulate it after the contract, i.e., they make a contract and then stipulate an option afterwards, they change the contract from the attribute of binding to something else. Then if increase and reduction are valid, they attach to the original contract, because the description of a thing subsists by it, not by itself. The benefit of attachment appears in tawliya, muraabaha, shuf'a, istihqaq, detention of the sold item, and invalidity of currency exchange. So one gives as tawliya and muraabaha on the whole in the case of increase, and on the remainder after reduction. The pre-emptor takes at what remains after reduction. And he may take without the increase only because it would nullify his established right, so they do not possess it. And the buyer has recourse against the seller for the whole. And if the rightful claimant validates the sale, he takes the whole, and he may detain it until he receives the increase. And if one sells dirhams for dirhams equally, then one of them increases or reduces and the other accepts
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the Hanafi ruling regarding price increase (ziyada) and decrease (hatt) after a sale, specifically in the context of muraabaha, tawliya, and shuf'a. It begins with a marginal note from Ya'qub Pasha highlighting a potential contradiction between al-Kafi and other sources regarding the validity of ziyada when the sold item is destroyed; the apparent narration rejects it while another accepts it. The main text then clarifies the consequences: the seller may detain the sold item until receiving both the original and additional price, and the buyer cannot withhold the increase when it is due. In case of hatt (reduction), the buyer pays the reduced amount. The passage then presents the objection of Zufar and al-Shafi'i: they argue that ziyada and hatt cannot attach to the original contract because the buyer already owns the item; increasing the price would be paying for one's own property, and reducing would leave the item without full consideration. They treat such adjustments as new gifts. The Hanafi response is based on the principle that modifying the description of a contract is easier than modifying its essence. Since parties have the authority to rescind entirely (iqala), they certainly have authority to change the description (from loss to profit, etc.). This is analogous to stipulating an option after the contract. Once valid, the adjustment attaches to the original contract, affecting tawliya (selling at cost), muraabaha (selling at profit), shuf'a (pre-emption), istihqaq (reclamation), and other areas. For example, in tawliya and muraabaha, one calculates based on the total price including the increase or after reduction. The pre-emptor takes at the reduced price, not including the increase because that would harm his right. The passage thus establishes the Hanafi position that such adjustments are not new contracts but modifications of the original, with significant practical implications.

Key Terms
زيادة (ziyāda): An increase in the price after the contract, either voluntarily or by agreement, which according to the Hanafis attaches to the original contract.
حط (ḥaṭṭ): A reduction or discount in the price after the contract, treated by Hanafis as a modification of the contract's description.
إقالة (iqāla): Mutual rescission of a sale contract, which the Hanafis use as a basis for argument that modifying the description is easier than full rescission.
وصف (waṣf): Description or attribute of the contract, such as being profitable, loss-making, or just. The Hanafis argue that changing the description is permissible because it is less than changing the essence (asl).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a price increase (ziyada) after the sale is concluded?
A) It is invalid and treated as a new gift.
B) It is valid and attaches to the original contract, affecting subsequent calculations like muraabaha.
C) It is valid only if the sold item is still in existence.
D) It is valid only with the agreement of both parties.

Key Takeaway
Price adjustments after sale are valid and attach to the original contract because modifying a contract's description is easier than annulling it.

Post-Contract Price Changes: Valid Modification of Description
Inter-madhhab dispute on post-contract price adjustments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on post-contract price increases and decreases, explain the evidence using the analogy of iqala, and apply the ruling to transactions like murabaha, tawliya, and shuf'a.

Core Idea
A post-sale price change is a change in the contract's description, not its essence; thus it is valid and attaches to the original contract.

In this passage, the Hanafi school defends the validity of increasing or decreasing the price after a sale contract has been concluded, against the objections of Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i. The key is understanding that such adjustments modify the description (profit/loss/break-even) of the contract, not its substance. The lesson will walk through the opposing arguments, the Hanafi response using qiyas with iqala, and the practical consequences for subsequent transactions.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school holds regarding post-contract price increase and decrease: both are valid and part of the original contract.
2
Identify the opposing view
State the positions of Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i: they consider increase and decrease as independent gifts, not part of the contract.
3
Follow the opposing proof
Understand their reasoning: increase means paying for what you already own; decrease leaves part of the item without consideration.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine the Hanafi argument: parties have authority to rescind (iqala), so they can change the description; changing description is easier than changing essence; analogy with adding an option after contract.
5
Determine thamarah al-khilaf
See how the difference affects practical transactions: tawliya, murabaha, shuf'a, etc. The pre-emptor takes only the lower amount.

البيع باب الإقالة والمرابحة والتولية
كما هو مذهب زُفَرَ له والشَّافِعِي )
[1] قوله : كما هو مذهب زفر والشافعي ؛ فإنَّ عندهما لا يصح الحط والمزيد، على اعتبار الالتحاق بأصل العقد، بل على اعتبار ابتداء الصلة ؛ لأنه لا يمكن تصحيحُ الزيادة ثمناً؛ لأنه يصير ملكه عوض ملكه ، فإنَّ المشتري ملك المبيع بالعقد بالمسمّى من الثمن قبل الزيادة. فلي زاد في الثمن مع ذلك، كانت الزيادة لأجل ملك نفسه، وهو المبيع، وذلك لا يجوز، فلا يلتحق الزيادة بأصل العقد، وكذلك الحطّ ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ الثمن صارَ مقابلاً بكل المبيع، فلا يمكن إخراجُ بعضه فلو أخرج بقي المبيعُ بلا عوض، وذا لا يجوز، ولا يمكن أن يجعل الباقي في مقابلة الكلّ ؛ لانتفاء العقد الجديد، فصار بُرا مبتدأ. ولنا : إنَّ البائع والمشتري بالخط والزيادة يغيران العقـد مـن وصف مشروع إلى وصف مشروع آخر، وهو كونه رابحاً أو خاسراً أو عدلاً، فإنَّ البيع شرع خاسراً ورابحاً وعادلاً، والزيادة في الثمن تجعل الخاسر عادلاً، والعادل رابحاً، والحط يجعل الرابح عادلاً، والعادل خاسراً. وللعاقدين ولاية رفع العقدِ بالإقالة محاولاً أن يكون لهما ولاية التغير من وصف إلى وصف ؛ لأنَّ التصرف في وصف الشيء أهون من التصرف في أصله، فالذي يملك التصرُّف في الأصل أولى بأن يملك التصرُّف في الوصف، وصار كما إذا أسقط الخيار أو شرطاه بعد العقد ، بأن عقدا عقداً ثمَّ شرطا الخيار بعده ، فقد يغير العقد من صفة اللزوم إلى غيره. ثم إذا صح الزيادة والحط فهما يلتحقان بأصل العقد ؛ لأنَّ وصف الشيء يقوم به لا بنفسه، وفائدة الالتحاق تظهر في التولية والمرابحة والشفعة والاستحقاق وحبس المبيع وفسادِ الصرف فيولي ويرابحُ على الكلِّ في الزيادة، وعلى الباقي بعد المحطوط، ويأخذ الشفيع بما بقي في الحط.
وإنما كان له أن يأخذ بدون الزيادة لما فيها من إبطال حقه الثابت، فلا يملكانه ، ويرجع المشتري على البائع بالكل، ولو أجاز المستحق البيع أخذ الكل، وله حبسه حتى يقبض الزيادة، ولو باع الدراهم بالدراهم متساوية ، ثمَّ زادَ أحدهما أو حط وقبل
فيرابح ويولي على الكلِّ إن زيد، وعلى ما بقي إن حط ، والشَّفيع يأخذ بالأقل في الفصلين، فلو قال يع عبدك من زيدٍ بألف على أني ضامن كذا من الثمن سوى الألف : ، أخذ الألف من زيدٍ والزيادة منه ، ولو لم يقل من الثَّمن، فالألفُ على زيد ولا شيء عليه. وكل دين أُجل إلى أجل معلوم
(فيرابح ويولي على الكلِّ إن زيد، وعلى ما بقي إن حط) (والشفيع يأخذ بالأقل في الفصلين ) : أي في الزّيادة على الثَّمنِ والحط عنه.

(فلو قال يع عبدك من زيدٍ بألف على أني ضامن كذا من الثَّمن سوى الألف ، أخذ الألف من زيدٍ والزّيادة منه، ولو لم يقل من الثَّمن، فالألفُ على زيد ولا شيء عليه ، وكلُّ دين أُجل إلى أجل معلوم " الآخر، وقبض الزائد في الزيادةِ أو المردودِ في الحط، فسد العقدُ كأنهما عقداه كذلك. الابتداء. هذا ما لخصته من كتب عديدة.

بأصل العقد.
[٢] قوله : في الفصلين ؛ وإن كان مقتضى الإلحاق بالأصل أن يأخذ الكل في صورة الزيادة ؛ لأنَّ حقه تعلَّق بالعقدِ الأوّل، وفي الزيادة إبطاله ، وليس لهما إبطاله. (۱) (مجمع الأنهر» (۲ : ۸۲).
Chapter on sale: section on rescission (iqala), cost-plus sale (murabaha), and assignment (tawliya). As is the madhhab of Zufar and al-Shafi'i: according to them, decrease (hatt) and increase (ziyada) are not valid as being attached to the original contract, but rather as independent gifts. Because it is not possible to validate the increase as a price, since it would make his ownership [of the price] in exchange for his own ownership; for the purchaser owned the sold item by the contract for the named price before the increase. If he then increased the price despite that, the increase would be for the sake of his own ownership, i.e. the sold item, and that is not permissible. So the increase does not attach to the original contract. Similarly for decrease: because all of the price became in exchange for the entire sold item, so it is not possible to remove part of it. If he removes it, the sold item remains without consideration, and that is not permissible. Nor can the remainder be made in exchange for the whole, because of the absence of a new contract, so it becomes a pure gift. And our evidence: the buyer and seller by decrease and increase change the contract from one valid description to another valid description, i.e. being profitable, loss-making, or break-even. For sale is legislated as loss-making, profitable, and break-even. An increase in price makes the loss-maker break-even, and the break-even profitable. A decrease makes the profitable break-even, and the break-even loss-making. And the contracting parties have the authority to rescind the contract by iqala, so likewise they have the authority to change from one description to another, because dealing with the description of a thing is easier than dealing with its essence. So whoever has authority over the essence is more entitled to have authority over the description. It becomes like when they drop an option or stipulate it after the contract: they contract, then stipulate an option afterward, thus changing the contract from binding to non-binding. Then if the increase and decrease are valid, they attach to the original contract, because the description of a thing is established by it, not by itself. The benefit of attachment appears in assignment (tawliya), cost-plus sale (murabaha), pre-emption (shuf'a), rightful claim (istiqhaq), detention of the sold item, and invalidity of currency exchange. So one assigns and sells at cost-plus on the entire amount if increased, and on the remainder after the decrease. And the pre-emptor takes what remains in case of decrease. Rather, he takes without the increase because the increase would nullify his established right, and they do not have authority to nullify it. And the purchaser takes back from the seller the entire price if the sold item is reclaimed. And if the rightful owner authorizes the sale, he takes the entire sold item, and has the right to detain it until the increase is paid. And if one sells silver coins for silver coins equal in weight, then one of them increases or decreases and the other accepts, then one assigns and sells at cost-plus on the entire amount if increased, and on what remains if decreased. And the pre-emptor takes the lesser in both cases. So if he says: 'Sell your slave from Zayd for one thousand on condition that I am guarantor of such and such of the price aside from the thousand,' he takes the thousand from Zayd and the increase from him. And if he did not say 'from the price,' then the thousand is upon Zayd and nothing upon him. And every debt deferred to a known term... (then commentary on these points).
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the validity of increasing or decreasing the price after a sale contract has been concluded. The text begins by stating the position of Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i: they consider any post-contract increase or decrease in the price as invalid as an attachment to the original contract. Instead, they view it as an independent gift because, in their reasoning, an increase would mean the buyer is paying for something he already owns, and a decrease would leave part of the sold item without consideration. The Hanafi school, represented here by the text, responds with a robust rational argument. The Hanafis argue that the buyer and seller are merely changing the description of the contract (from loss-making to break-even, etc.), not its essence. Since sale contracts are legislated in various forms (profitable, loss-making, break-even), a change in price shifts the contract from one valid description to another. Moreover, the parties already have the authority to rescind the entire contract (iqala), so they certainly have the authority to change its description, as dealing with a description is easier than dealing with the essence. This analogy is supported by cases where an option is added after the contract, changing its binding nature. Consequently, if the increase or decrease is valid, it becomes part of the original contract, affecting later transactions like tawliya (assignment), murabaha (cost-plus sale), and shuf'a (pre-emption). The text further elaborates on specific applications: the pre-emptor takes only the lower amount (the original price in case of decrease, and without the increase), because the increase would nullify his established right. Additional scenarios include a guarantor of part of the price and deferment of debt. The Hanafi position is that all such adjustments are valid and integrated into the original contract.

Key Terms
الزيادة (al-ziyada): An increase in the price after the sale contract has been concluded.
الحط (al-hatt): A decrease in the price after the sale contract has been concluded.
التولية (al-tawliya): Assignment of a purchase to another party at the original cost price, without profit.
المرابحة (al-murabaha): A sale where the seller discloses the cost price and adds a profit margin.
الشفعة (al-shuf'a): Pre-emption right of a co-owner to purchase the sold share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is an increase in price after the sale contract considered part of the original contract rather than a new independent gift?
A) Because the parties have the authority to rescind the entire contract, so changing a description (which is easier) is also within their authority.
B) Because the increase is a separate contract that must be explicitly agreed upon.
C) Because the increase is obligatory as a form of charity.
D) Because the decrease is invalid, and only increase is valid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a post-contract price change is valid and integrates into the original contract because it modifies the contract's description, not its essence.

Preemptor's Right in Murabahah: The Lesser Price Rule
Ruling with proof on preemptor's right in murabahah/tawliyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on how profit and discount in murabahah/tawliyah affect the buyer and the preemptor, and determine what the preemptor takes in each case.

Core Idea
The preemptor's right attaches to the original contract price; any increase or discount does not enlarge or reduce his right beyond the lesser amount.

When a buyer purchases an item on behalf of a partner or later sells it, the profit or discount must be distributed fairly. The preemptor (shafi‘) has a right to step into the buyer's shoes at the contract price. But what happens if the price later increases or a discount is given? This lesson explains the Hanafi rule: the preemptor always takes the lesser of the two amounts, balancing the rights of all parties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage addresses two situations: when the price increases (ziyādah) and when it decreases (khaṭṭ/discount).
The ruling applies to both murabahah (sale with stated profit) and tawliyah (sale at cost).
2
Determine the effect on the buyer's and seller's accounts
If the price increases, the seller takes profit on the entire original price. If the price decreases, the seller takes only what remains after the discount.
The discount is considered attached to the original contract.
3
Apply the preemptor's right
The preemptor takes the lesser amount in both cases: if the price increased, he takes the original price (not the increased amount); if it decreased, he takes the decreased price.
This is because his right was attached to the first contract/price.
4
Understand the legal reasoning
The discount attaches to the original contract, so it reduces the price the preemptor must pay. The increase cannot nullify the preemptor's right, so he is not forced to pay more than the original price.
The reasoning is based on attachment (ilḥāq) to the original contract.
5
Note the nuance
Although attachment to the original contract might suggest the preemptor takes the increased price, his specific right is limited to the first price, so the increase is not binding on him.
This shows the preemptor's right is protected from subsequent changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيرابح ويولي على الكلِّ إن زيد، وعلى ما بقي إن حط ، والشَّفيع يأخذ بالأقل في الفصلين،

(فيرابح ويولي على الكلِّ إن زيد، وعلى ما بقي إن حط) (والشفيع يأخذ بالأقل في الفصلين ) : أي في الزّيادة على الثَّمنِ والحط عنه.
الثابت.
أما في الحط ؛ فلانه التحق بأصل العقد.
أما في الزيادة ؛ فلأنَّ حقَّهُ تعلَّق بالشَّمنِ الأَوَّلِ، فلا يملك الغيرُ إبطال حقه

[١] قوله : فيرابح ... الخ ؛ هذا تفريع على صحة الزيادة والحط وعلى إلحاقهما بأصل العقد.
[٢] قوله : في الفصلين ؛ وإن كان مقتضى الإلحاق بالأصل أن يأخذ الكل في صورة الزيادة ؛ لأنَّ حقه تعلَّق بالعقدِ الأوّل، وفي الزيادة إبطاله ، وليس لهما إبطاله.
He sells at profit and gives ownership on the whole if it increased, and on what remains if it decreased. And the preemptor takes the lesser in both cases: that is, in the increase over the price and the discount from it. Established. As for the discount, because it attaches to the original contract. As for the increase, because his right was attached to the first price, so another cannot nullify his right. [1] His statement: 'He sells at profit... etc.'; this is a branch on the validity of increase and discount and on their attachment to the original contract. [2] His statement: 'in both cases'; even though the requirement of attachment to the original is that he takes all in the case of increase, because his right was attached to the first contract, and in the increase is its nullification, and they cannot nullify it.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage addresses the distribution of profit (ribh) and discount (wadi'ah) in murabahah and tawliyah sales, and the right of the preemptor (shafi'). The ruling states that the seller who is selling on behalf of a partner takes profit on the entire price if the price increased, or takes only the remaining amount if the price decreased. The preemptor takes the lesser amount in both situations: if the price increased, he takes the original price (not the increased amount), and if decreased, he takes the decreased price. The reasoning is that the discount attaches to the original contract, so it affects the original price, while the increase cannot nullify the preemptor's right that was attached to the original price. The footnotes explain that this is a derivation from the validity of increase and discount, and that although attachment would suggest the preemptor takes all in the case of increase, his right is specifically attached to the first contract, so the increase does not affect him.

Key Terms
مرابحة (murābaḥah): A sale in which the seller discloses the cost and adds a specified profit.
تولية (tawliyah): A sale at the original cost price without profit.
شفيع (shafī‘): The preemptor who has the right to take the property from the buyer at the contract price.
حط (khaṭṭ): A discount or reduction from the original price.
زيادة (ziyādah): An increase over the original price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The increased price
B) The original price
C) The lesser of the two
D) The greater of the two

Key Takeaway
The preemptor takes the lesser price in murabahah/tawliyah: the original price if it increased, the discounted price if it decreased, because his right attaches to the first contract and discount, but increase does not nullify his right.

Guarantee of Part of Price in a Sale
Ruling on guarantee of part of price in a sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two scenarios regarding guarantee of part of the price and determine liability based on the guarantor's wording.

Core Idea
The guarantor's wording determines liability: 'from the price' makes him liable; 'besides' severs his liability.

This lesson clarifies a subtle but crucial distinction in sale transactions where a third party guarantees part of the price. The ruling hinges on whether the guarantor explicitly attaches his guarantee to the price or simply promises an additional payment. Understanding this distinction prevents disputes over liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and note the two cases: (1) when the guarantor says 'I guarantee such and such from the price besides the thousand,' and (2) when he does not say 'from the price.'
The key phrase is 'min al-thaman' (from the price).
2
Determine liability in each scenario
In the first case, the guarantor is liable for the excess over the thousand; the buyer pays the thousand. In the second case, the buyer pays the thousand and the guarantor owes nothing.
The ruling clearly states: 'he takes the thousand from Zayd and the increase from him' vs. 'the thousand is upon Zayd and nothing upon him.'
3
Analyze the reasoning behind the second scenario
The commentary addresses an objection: how can the guarantor owe nothing when his statement is explicit in guarantee? The answer: the basis of his speech is that he said 'I guarantee besides the thousand,' so the guarantee is not attached to the price.
The guarantee is considered a separate promise, not a liability on the price.
4
Identify the condition for guarantor's liability
The condition for the guarantor to be liable is that he explicitly says 'from the price' (min al-thaman). Without this, he is not liable for any part of the price.
This is a condition of validity for the guarantee to be attached to the price.
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Given a similar case, determine liability by first checking the wording: does it include 'min al-thaman' or not? Then apply the appropriate ruling.
Practice with the practice tasks below.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو قال يع عبدك من زيدٍ بألف على أني ضامن كذا من الثمن سوى الألف : ، أخذ الألف من زيدٍ والزيادة منه ، ولو لم يقل من الثَّمن، فالألفُ على زيد ولا شيء عليه.

(فلو قال يع عبدك من زيدٍ بألف على أني ضامن كذا من الثَّمن سوى الألف ، أخذ الألف من زيدٍ والزّيادة منه، ولو لم يقل من الثَّمن، فالألفُ على زيد ولا شيء عليه)

[٣] قوله : ولا شيء عليه ؛ فإن قيل : فكيف لا شيء عليه، وعبارته صريحة في الضمان، قلنا : مبنى الكلام على أنه قال : بع عبدك من زيد على أني ضامن سوى الألف، فالضمانُ إذن غيرُ متعلّق بالثمن، فلا شيء عليه من الثمن، كذا في «مجمع الأنهر).
If he said, 'Sell your slave to Zayd for one thousand on condition that I guarantee such and such of the price besides the thousand,' he takes the thousand from Zayd and the increase from him. And if he did not say 'from the price,' the thousand is upon Zayd and nothing upon him (the guarantor). [3] His statement: 'and nothing upon him'; if it is said: how is nothing upon him, and his statement is explicit in guarantee? We say: the basis of the speech is that he said: 'Sell your slave to Zayd on condition that I guarantee besides the thousand,' so the guarantee is therefore not attached to the price, so nothing is upon him from the price, as in Majma' al-Anhur.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a person guarantees a portion of the price in a sale. The ruling distinguishes between two cases: if the guarantor explicitly says he guarantees a certain amount "from the price" (min al-thaman), then he is liable for the price from Zayd (the buyer) and the extra amount from himself. But if he does not say "from the price," then he is not liable for anything beyond the thousand from Zayd. The commentary explains that in the second case, the guarantee is not attached to the price, so the guarantor owes nothing. This is based on the wording: if he says "I guarantee besides the thousand," the guarantee is separate from the price.

Key Terms
ضامن (ḍāmin): Guarantor; one who undertakes liability for a debt or obligation.
ثمن (thaman): Price; the monetary consideration in a sale.
سوى (siwā): Besides; in addition to.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The guarantor
C) The seller
D) No one

Key Takeaway
The guarantor's liability for part of the price depends on explicitly saying 'from the price'; otherwise, he owes nothing.
