Deferment of Debts: General Validity and Loan Exception
Ruling with proof on debt deferment and loan exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on deferring debts, distinguish between general debts and loans, and explain why loan deferment is invalid due to riba.

Core Idea
Any debt can be deferred except a loan—loan deferment becomes riba.

In this lesson, we examine a key ruling from the Hanafi school on deferring debts. While the general principle is that a creditor may postpone repayment of any debt to a known future term, an important exception exists for loans (qard). Deferring a loan essentially creates a credit sale of money for money, which falls under riba. We will explore the reasoning and the proof behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon: the act of deferring a debt to a known term. Then identify the general ruling: it is valid (sahih) for all debts except loans.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the evidence cited for the exception: the principle 'al-naqdu khayrun min al-nasi'ah' (cash is better than credit) and the analogy that loan deferment becomes a sale of dirhams on credit, which is riba.
The proof is based on a hadith-derived maxim.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the proof connects to the ruling: deferring a loan gives the debtor an extension, which in a loan of the same genus (e.g., dirhams for dirhams) constitutes a forbidden increase due to delay, i.e., riba.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Understand that this is a disputed issue across madhhabs. The Hanafi position is strict on loans; other schools may allow deferment under certain conditions. The text also mentions a unanimous agreement on the permissibility of loans themselves despite the apparent riba issue.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The mufta bihi position is that loan deferment is invalid. This is based on the clear analogy to riba and the need to preserve the integrity of loan contracts as acts of charity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل دين أُجل إلى أجل معلوم صح إلا القرض

وكلُّ دين أُجل إلى أجل معلوم " الآخر، وقبض الزائد في الزيادةِ أو المردودِ في الحط، فسد العقدُ كأنهما عقداه كذلك. الابتداء. هذا ما لخصته من كتب عديدة.

[1] قوله : صح؛ أي ذلك التأجيل، وإن كان حالاً في الأصل؛ لأنَّ المطالبة حقه فله أن يؤخر سواء كان ثمن مبيع أو غيره تيسيراً على من له عليه، ألا ترى أنه يملك إبراء الثمن عمَّن عليه مطلقاً، فكذا يملك البراءة المؤقتة إلى أجل ولا بدَّ من قبوله ممن عليه الدين ، فلو لم يقبله بطل التأخير، فيكون حالاً ، ويصح تعليق التأجيل بالشرط ، لما في «البحر»(۱).

[2] قوله : إلا القرض؛ استثناء من قوله : صح؛ أي لا يصح تأجيله لكونه إعارة وصلة ابتداءً ومعاوضةٌ انتهاء، فعلى الاعتبار الأول لا يلزم التأجيل فيه ، كما في الإعارة، إذ لو لزم الأجل لصارَ جبراً على المشتري، وهو المكـث عـن المطالبة بمضي الأجل، ولا جبر في التبرع. وعلى الاعتبار الثاني : لا يصح؛ لأنه يصير بيعُ الدراهم بالدراهم نسيئة، فلا يجوز ؛ لكونه ربا ؛ لأنَّ النقد خير من النسيئة، وهو ظاهر ، وهذا يقتضي فساد القرض، لكن ندب الشرع إليه، وأجمع الأمة على جوازه لكثرة الحاجة إليه، ولا يذهب عليك أن ما ذكر الشارح له من الدليل هو باعتبار آخر الاعتبارين، وتمامه ما ذكرناه ، فتدبر.

[3] قوله : فلا يجوز؛ نعم إذا أوصى أن يقرض من مالِهِ فلاناً إلى سنته يجوز من الثلث، ويلزم التأجيل، ولا يطالب حتى تمضي المدة؛ لأنه وصية بالتبرع، والوصية يتسامح فيها نظراً للموصى، ألا ترى أنها تجوز بالخدمة والسكني، فتلزم حقاً للموصى.
Every debt deferred to a known term is valid except a loan. And every debt deferred to a known term, and the receipt of the excess in the increase or the returned amount in the discount, the contract becomes invalid as if they had contracted it that way from the beginning. This is what I summarized from many books. [1] His statement: 'is valid'; that is, that deferment, even if it was originally due, because the right to demand is his, so he may postpone, whether it is a price of a sale or other, to ease on the one who owes him. Do you not see that he has the right to absolve the price from the debtor absolutely, so likewise he has the right to temporary absolution to a term, and it is necessary that the debtor accepts it; if he does not accept it, the postponement is void, so it becomes due. And it is valid to condition the deferment, as in al-Bahr. [2] His statement: 'except a loan'; exception from his statement 'is valid'; meaning its deferment is not valid because it is a loan (i'arah) and a connection (silah) initially and a mu'awadah (exchange) eventually. According to the first consideration, deferment is not binding in it, as in loan (i'arah), because if the term were binding, it would become compulsion on the buyer, and he would refrain from demanding after the term, and there is no compulsion in voluntary acts. According to the second consideration, it is not valid because it becomes a sale of dirhams for dirhams on credit, so it is not permissible because it is riba; because cash is better than credit, and this is apparent. This would entail the invalidity of the loan, but the Shari'ah encouraged it, and the ummah unanimously agreed on its permissibility due to great need. And do not overlook that what the commentator mentioned of the proof is according to the last of the two considerations, and its completion is what we mentioned, so contemplate. [3] His statement: 'so it is not permissible'; yes, if one bequeaths that a loan be given to so-and-so from his wealth for a year, it is permissible from the one-third, and the deferment is binding, and he cannot be demanded until the period expires, because it is a bequest of a voluntary act, and bequests are tolerated in consideration of the testator. Do you not see that it is permissible for service and residence, so it becomes a binding right for the testator.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage covers the general ruling that deferring any debt to a known term is valid, except for loans (qard). The reasoning is that loan deferment effectively creates a sale of money for money on credit, which is riba. The commentary explains that the creditor has the right to demand or postpone, and if the debtor accepts, it is valid. However, for loans, deferment is invalid because a loan is voluntary (i'arah) initially and an exchange at the end; thus binding deferment would be compulsion. The exception is if the loan is made under a bequest (wasiyyah), then deferment is valid from the one-third of the estate. The text also notes that the ummah agreed on the permissibility of loans despite the apparent invalidity due to riba, because of need.

Key Terms
دين (dayn): A debt; a liability to pay money or goods.
أجل (ajal): A fixed term or deadline for payment.
قرض (qarḍ): A loan; a contract where one gives money to another to be repaid later.
ربا (ribā): Usury; illegal increase in exchange of same genus goods.
النقد خير من النسيئة (al-naqdu khayrun min al-nasi'ah): A legal maxim meaning that cash payment is better than credit, used to argue against deferment in loans.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the debtor may not accept
B) Because it becomes a sale of money for money on credit, which is riba
C) Because the loan is a gift
D) Because the term must be known

Key Takeaway
General debts can be deferred to a known term, but loan deferment is invalid because it constitutes riba al-nasi'ah.

Invalidity of Deferment in a Loan (Qard)
Exception of loan from validity of deferment with reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on deferring a loan and explain why it constitutes riba.

Core Idea
Deferring a loan creates a credit sale of currency, which is forbidden riba.

In Hanafi fiqh, deferring the repayment of a debt is generally permissible as a kindness to the debtor. However, an important exception is the loan (qard). This lesson unpacks why stipulating a term in a loan is invalid, how it transforms into a prohibited riba transaction, and the narrow exception for bequests. Understanding this ruling sharpens your awareness of the subtle boundaries between permissible deferment and usury.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is stipulating a fixed term (ta'jil) in a loan contract. The Hanafi ruling is that this stipulation is invalid (la yasihh) — it does not take effect. The loan remains due immediately upon demand.
The invalidity means the condition is void, not the entire loan.
2
Understand the dual nature of qard
Qard has two aspects: initially it is a donation (i'arah) — the lender gives benefit without immediate compensation. At the end, it becomes an exchange (mu'awadah) — the borrower must repay the equivalent. The ruling uses the latter aspect: deferment turns this exchange into a credit sale of money.
The first consideration would make deferment non-binding, but the second renders it invalid due to riba.
3
Examine the proof: Qiyas to riba
If repayment is deferred, the lender gives cash now and receives cash later. That is a sale of dirhams for dirhams on credit (bai' al-darahim bi al-darahim nasi'ah), which is prohibited riba. The text cites the principle: 'al-naqd khayr min al-nasi'ah' (cash is better than credit). Any excess created by deferment is riba.
The proof is 'aqli (rational qiyas) based on the risk of excess.
4
Note the exception: bequest
If a testator bequeaths a loan with a fixed term from one-third of his estate, the deferment becomes binding. Bequests are lenient because they benefit the testator (e.g., reward). This does not violate the general rule because the bequest itself is a gratuitous transfer, not a loan contract with a stipulated term.
This exception does not undermine the general prohibition; it is a special dispensation.
5
Appreciate the wisdom
The Shari'ah encourages giving loans as charity (qard hasan). But stipulating a term would create an unequal exchange — the lender gets a future claim of the same amount, which is inferior to cash today. This is riba. The prohibition protects justice and prevents exploitation.
The ruling does not discourage loans; it preserves their charitable nature.

Source Text

يجوز كحصاد الزرع ونحوه يجوز ، كما جاز ذلك في الكفالة. [١] قوله : صح ؛ أي ذلك التأجيل، وإن كان حالاً في الأصل ؛ لأنَّ المطالبة حقه فله أن يؤخر سواء كان ثمن مبيع أو غيره تيسيراً على من له عليه، ألا ترى أنه يملك إبراء الثمن عمَّن عليه مطلقاً ، فكذا يملك البراءة المؤقتة إلى أجل ولا بدَّ من قبوله ممن عليه الدين ، فلو لم يقبله بطل التأخير، فيكون حالاً ، ويصح تعليق التأجيل بالشرط ، لما في «البحر»(١). ٢) قوله : إلا القرض ؛ استثناء من قوله : صح؛ أي لا يصح تأجيله لكونه إعارة وصلة ابتداءً ومعاوضةٌ انتهاء، فعلى الاعتبار الأول لا يلزم التأجيل فيه ، كما في الإعارة، إذ لو لزم الأجل لصارَ جبراً على المشتري، وهو المكـث عـن المطالبة بمضي الأجل، ولا جبر في التبرع. وعلى الاعتبار الثاني : لا يصح؛ لأنه يصير بيعُ الدراهم بالدراهم نسيئة، فلا يجوز ؛ لكونه ربا ؛ لأنَّ النقد خير من النسيئة، وهو ظاهر ، وهذا يقتضي فساد القرض، لكن ندب الشرع إليه، وأجمع الأمة على جوازه لكثرة الحاجة إليه، ولا يذهب عليك أن ما ذكر الشارح له من الدليل هو باعتبار آخر الاعتبارين، وتمامه ما ذكرناه ، فتدبر. [٣] قوله : فلا يجوز ؛ نعم إذا أوصى أن يقرض من مالِهِ فلاناً إلى سنته يجوز من الثلث، ويلزم التأجيل، ولا يطالب حتى تمضي المدة؛ لأنه وصية بالتبرع، والوصية يتسامح فيها نظراً للموصى، ألا ترى أنها تجوز بالخدمة والسكني، فتلزم حقاً للموصى.

It is permissible like harvesting crops and the like, as is permitted in suretyship. Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the commentary on al-Wiqayah, expanding on the exception of loan (qard) from the general validity of deferment. The commentator explains that deferring repayment of a loan is invalid because it effectively becomes a sale of currency on credit, which is prohibited riba. The reasoning is that cash is better than credit (al-naqd khayr min al-nasi'ah), so any deferment that creates a difference in value is riba. The text notes that the loan contract itself is encouraged by Shari'ah and unanimously permitted, but the stipulation of a term is invalid. However, if a testator bequeaths a loan with a term, that bequest is valid from one-third of the estate, and the term becomes binding, because bequests are given leeway for the benefit of the testator. The passage also clarifies that the general validity of deferment applies to debts like the price in a sale, but not to loans.

Key Terms
القرض (al-qard): A loan given to someone with the obligation to repay the equivalent.
التأجيل (al-ta'jil): Postponing the due date of a debt or consideration to a future fixed term.
ربا (riba): An excess in exchange without a valid countervalue, especially in monetary transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

In Hanafi fiqh, a loan cannot be deferred because it becomes a credit sale of currency, which is riba; the loan remains due immediately.

Defining Riba: The Hanafi Legal Definition
Defines riba legally as an excess without countervalue stipulated in a contract.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of riba and identify its three essential components.

Core Idea
Riba is an excess stipulated in a contract that is free from any countervalue.

Riba (usury) is a central prohibited element in Islamic finance. This passage opens the chapter on riba by providing a precise legal definition that distinguishes it from lawful profit. Understanding this definition is crucial for identifying prohibited transactions in sales, loans, and other exchanges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic background
Note that riba linguistically means any increase, but legally it is restricted to a specific type. The author clarifies that the legal term is not absolute increase, as markets exist for making profit.
The Arabic root is ra-ba-wa, and the relative adjective is 'ribawi'.
2
Deconstruct the legal definition
Break down the phrase 'fadlun khalu 'an 'iwadin' (excess devoid of countervalue). This yields three components: (1) an excess (fadl), (2) absence of legitimate countervalue ('iwad), and (3) stipulation in the contract.
The excess must be part of the contract's terms, not a voluntary gift.
3
Understand the stipulation condition
The excess must be 'stipulated for one of the contracting parties' (shurita li ahad al-'aqidayn). This excludes cases where an excess arises inadvertently or as a gift without prior condition.
Al-Islah refines this to 'for one of the two substitutes' to cover agents.
4
Refine the parties involved
Consider why al-Islah says 'for one of the two substitutes' instead of 'for one of the contracting parties'. This ensures that the excess is evaluated from the perspective of the actual seller/buyer, not a nominal agent.
This refinement protects against circumvention of riba through intermediaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والربا - بكسر الراء المهملة، والقصر اسم من الرَّبو بالفتح، والسكون، فلامه واو، ولذا قيل في النسبة ربوي، وفتحها خطأ، ومنه الأشياء الربوية.
قال في «المصباح المنير») : الرِّبا : الفضلُ والزيادة ، وهو مقصور على الأشهر، ويثني : ربوان، بالواو على الأصل، وقد يقال : ربيان على التخفيف، وينسب إليه على لفظه فيقال : ربوي ، قاله أبو عبيدة الله وغيره، وزاد المطرزي ) ، فقال : الفتح في النسبة خطأ. انتهى. وقياسه أن يكتب بالألف كما تقرر في علم الخط أنَّ الألف الثالثة إن كانت عن واو يكتب بالألف، وقد جرت العادة بأن يكتب بالواو بعدها ألف تشبهاً بواو الجمع، وإنما هو خط المصحف، ذكره العلامة البرْجَندِي .

Chapter of Riba: It is an excess devoid of countervalue stipulated for one of the contracting parties. [1] His statement: 'Chapter of Riba'; the connection to the preceding chapter on Murabaha is clear, that in both Murabaha and Riba there is an increase, except that increase is halal and this is haram, and ignorance is the default in matters, so he presented what relates to that increase over what relates to this. And riba - with a kasra on the ra', shortened, is derived from al-rabw with a fatha and sukun, its lam is waw, so the relative adjective is 'ribawi', and opening the ra' is an error, and from it are usurious items. It is said in al-Misbah al-Munir: Riba: is excess and increase, and it is shortened in the famous usage, dual: ribawan with waw on the original, and it may be said ribiyan with lightening, and the relative is formed on its pronunciation: ribawi, said by Abu Ubaydah and others. Al-Mutarrizi added: The fatha in the relative is an error. End. And by analogy it should be written with alif as established in orthography that the third alif if from waw is written with alif, but convention is to write it with waw followed by alif resembling the waw of the plural, and it is the orthography of the Mushaf. Mentioned by al-Birjandi. [2] His statement: 'It is an excess...' Since riba linguistically is excess and increase, one says raba al-mal meaning it increased, and it is apparent that what is meant is not absolute excess and increase by consensus; because markets in Muslim lands are opened for gaining excess and profit, but rather what is meant is a specific excess, so the author defined it by saying: it is an excess devoid... [3] His statement: 'for one of the contracting parties' meaning the sellers, lenders, or pawnbrokers. And in al-Islah: for one of the two substitutes, and he did not say 'for one of the contracting parties' because the contractor may be an agent or an unauthorized person, and the consideration is that the excess is for the seller and buyer. End.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on riba (usury) in the commentary. The author first justifies placing this chapter after Murabaha, since both involve increase but one is halal and the other haram. He gives a linguistic and orthographic note on the word 'riba', confirming it is from the root ra-ba-wa and the relative is 'ribawi'. Then he explains the definition: riba is an excess (fadl) that is free from any countervalue ('iwad) and is stipulated for one of the contracting parties. This distinguishes it from lawful profit. The commentary notes that although riba linguistically means any increase, legal riba is a specific excess. The phrase 'for one of the contracting parties' is further refined: according to al-Islah, it should be 'for one of the two substitutes' to include cases where agents or unauthorized persons contract, as the real concern is the benefit to the actual seller or buyer.

Key Terms
ربا (riba): A legally prohibited excess in exchange that is stipulated without a valid countervalue.
فضل (fadl): Excess or increase in amount or value.
عوض ('iwad): Countervalue, consideration, or compensation in a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Riba is only in loans, while profit is in sales.
D) Riba involves interest, while profit does not.

Key Takeaway
Riba is legally defined as an excess stipulated in a contract that is free from any countervalue.

Definition of Riba in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines riba with commentary on each element.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of riba and explain each of its four essential elements.

Core Idea
Riba is a stipulated surplus devoid of compensation in an exchange — not any profit.

The Hanafi school defines riba precisely to distinguish prohibited excess from permissible profit. This lesson unpacks each element of that definition, showing what makes a transaction fall into the forbidden category and what keeps it permissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Locate the complete definition of riba in the passage: 'It is a surplus devoid of compensation, stipulated for one of the two contracting parties in an exchange.' Memorize this definition.
The definition consists of four components: surplus, devoid of compensation, stipulated, and in exchange.
2
Analyze each component
Examine each component: 'surplus' (فضل) excludes ordinary profit; 'devoid of compensation' (خال عن عوض) excludes cases where the surplus is offset by a countervalue; 'stipulated' (شرط) excludes voluntary gifts after contract; 'in exchange' (في المعاوضة) includes sales and compensated gifts.
The commentary provides examples for each component.
3
Apply to borderline cases
Use the definition to evaluate transactions like a gift with a condition of service or buying ten dirhams with ten plus a daniq given as a gift. Determine if each satisfies all four conditions of riba.
The gift with service: the surplus is compensated (service), so not riba. The daniq gift: not stipulated, so not riba.
4
Contrast with other schools
Note that the Hanafi definition is narrow; other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may include any increase in a loan or barter without requiring stipulation. This difference affects what is considered prohibited riba.
Cross-madhhab understanding is useful for comparative fiqh.

[1] قوله : باب الربا ؛ وجه مناسبته لما تقدّم من باب المرابحة ظاهر، وهو أنَّ في كلِّ من المرابحة والربا زيادة، إلا أنَّ تلك الزيادة حلال، وهذه حرام، والجهل هو الأصل في الأشياء ، فقدم ما يتعلق بتلك الزيادة على ما يتعلق بهذه.
والربا - بكسر الراء المهملة، والقصر اسم من الرَّبو بالفتح، والسكون، فلامه واو، ولذا قيل في النسبة ربوي، وفتحها خطأ، ومنه الأشياء الربوية.
قال في «المصباح المنير» : الرِّبا : الفضلُ والزيادة ، وهو مقصور على الأشهر، ويثني : ربوان، بالواو على الأصل، وقد يقال : ربيان على التخفيف، وينسب إليه على لفظه فيقال : ربوي ، قاله أبو عبيدة الله وغيره، وزاد المطرزي ، فقال : الفتح في النسبة خطأ. انتهى. وقياسه أن يكتب بالألف كما تقرر في علم الخط أنَّ الألف الثالثة إن كانت عن واو يكتب بالألف، وقد جرت العادة بأن يكتب بالواو بعدها ألف تشبهاً بواو الجمع، وإنما هو خط المصحف، ذكره العلامة البرْجَندِي .
[٢] قوله : هو فضل ...الخ ؛ لما كان الربا في اللغة: الفضلُ والزيادة، يقال: ربي المال أي زاد، والظاهر أنه ليس المراد مطلق الفضل والزيادة بالإجماع ؛ لأنَّ الأسواق في بلاد المسلمين إنّما فتحت للاستفضال والاسترباح، بل المراد منه فضل مخصوص، فعرفه المصنف بقوله : هو فضل خال ... الخ.
[٣]قوله : لأحد العاقدين ؛ أي البائعين والمقرضين أو الـــراهنين ، وفي «الإصلاح» : لأحد البدلين ، ولم يقل : لأحد العاقدين ؛ لأن العاقد قد يكون وكيلاً وقد يكون فضولياً، والمعتبر كون الفضل للبائع والمشتري. انتهى. لكن عقد الوكيل لما كان عقداً للموكل، وعقدُ الفضولي يتوقف على قبول المالك، فيصير العاقد حقيقةً الموكل أو المالك، فلا حاجة إلى التبديل.

في المعاوضة
[١]قوله : في المعاوضة ؛ وإنما لم يقل : في البيع، ليتناول الهبة بشرط العوض، فإن حكمها بعد القبض حكم البيع. كذا في شرح (النقاية» للبِرْجَنْدِيّ
[٢] قوله : لا يكون من باب الربا : قال العلامة الشامي : وهذا مبني على ما حققناه من أن البيوع الفاسدة ليست كلّها من الربا، بل ما فيه شرط فاسد فيه نفع لأحد العاقدين، فافهم. انتهى.
[٣] قوله : الفضل ؛ كما لو قال : وهبتك كذا بشرط أن تخدمني شهراً، فإنَّ هذا شرط فاسد لا تبطل الهبة به، كما تقرر في موضعه، وإن اشترى عشرة دراهم فضة بعشرة دراهم، وزاده دانقاً [إن] وهبه منه انعدم الربا، ولم يفسد الشراء، وهذا إن ضرَّها الكسر ؛ لأنها هبة مشاع لا يقسم، كذا في «المنح» نقلاً عن «الذخيرة» عن محمد .
Chapter of Riba
It is a surplus devoid of compensation, stipulated for one of the two contracting parties in an exchange.
[1] His statement: Chapter of Riba; the reason for its connection to what preceded from the chapter of Murabahah is apparent, and it is that in both Murabahah and Riba there is an increase, except that that increase is halal, and this is haram, and ignorance is the original principle in things, so he gave precedence to what pertains to that increase over what pertains to this.
And Riba - with kasra of the important ra', and it is a verbal noun from al-rabw with fatha and sukun, so its lam is waw, and due to that one says in the relative adjective ribawi, and its fatha is a mistake, and from it are ribawi items.
He said in al-Misbah al-Munir: al-riba: surplus and increase, and it is diptote according to the more famous, and it is dualized as ribawan with waw on the original, and it may be said rabiyan on the pattern of lightening, and relative adjective is formed from its pronunciation so it is said ribawi, said by Abu Ubaydah and others, and al-Mutarrizi added, saying: the fatha in the relative adjective is a mistake. End. And its analogy is that it be written with an alif as established in the science of orthography that the third alif if it originates from waw is written with alif, and the custom has gone that it is written with waw followed by alif on the analogy of the waw of the plural, and it is merely the writing of the Mus-haf, mentioned by al-Allamah al-Burjandi.
[2] His statement: 'a surplus...' etc.; since Raba in the language is surplus and increase, one says raba al-mal i.e. it increased, and apparently it is not intended as absolute surplus and increase by consensus, because markets in the land of Muslims were only opened for profit and gain, but rather intended is a specific surplus, so the author defined it by his saying: 'it is a surplus devoid...' etc.
[3] His statement: 'for one of the two contracting parties'; i.e. the sellers and the lenders or the pawnbrokers, and in al-Islah: for one of the two substitutes, and he did not say 'for one of the two contracting parties' because the contracting party may be an agent or a usurper, and the consideration is that the surplus be for the seller and the buyer. End. But the contract of an agent since it is a contract for the principal, and the contract of an usurper is suspended upon acceptance of the owner, so the true contracting party becomes the principal or the owner, so there is no need for substitution.
In the exchange
[1] His statement: 'in the exchange'; and he did not say 'in sale' in order to include a gift with the condition of compensation, for its ruling after possession is the ruling of sale. Thus in the Sharh al-Niqayah by al-Burjandi.
[2] His statement: 'it is not from the chapter of riba': said al-Allamah al-Shami: And this is based on what we have verified that invalid sales are not all from riba, but rather it is that which has an invalid condition containing benefit for one of the contracting parties, so understand. End.
[3] His statement: 'the surplus': such as if he said: I give this to you as a gift on condition that you serve me for a month, then this is an invalid condition that does not invalidate the gift, as established in its place. And if he bought ten silver dirhams with ten dirhams, and added a daniq, if he gave it as a gift from him, riba is eliminated and the purchase is not invalidated, and this is if the division harms because it is an undivided gift that cannot be divided. Thus in al-Minah quoting from al-Dhakhirah from Muhammad.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the definition of riba (usury/interest) from the matn of al-Wiqayah. The author first explains the placement of this chapter after the chapter on murabahah (cost-plus sale): both involve an increase, but murabahah's increase is permissible while riba's is forbidden. Since the default in matters is permissibility (the original principle being that things are permissible unless proven otherwise), the permissible increase is discussed first. Then, the linguistic meaning of riba is explained: it is surplus and increase, and the word is derived from rabw (increase). The commentary notes the correct vowelization and relative adjective. The core of the definition is then unpacked: ربا is a stipulated surplus (فضل) that is devoid of compensation (خال عن عوض) and is stipulated for one of the contracting parties (شرط لأحد العاقدين) in an exchange (في المعاوضة). Each phrase is explained. 'Surplus' excludes mere profit from trade which is permissible. 'Devoid of compensation' excludes cases where the surplus is offset by a countervalue of a different genus, like exchanging inferior grain for superior grain of a different type. 'Stipulated for one of the contracting parties' excludes gifts where surplus is not stipulated for either party. 'In exchange' includes sale and also gift with compensation, which after possession is treated like a sale. The commentary also addresses invalid sales: not every invalid sale involves riba, only those with a condition that benefits one party. Examples illustrate: a gift with a condition of service does not become riba because the service is not a commodity; a purchase with an extra daniq given as a gift is permissible because the extra is not part of the exchange. This meticulous definition shows how Hanafi fiqh precisely delineates prohibited riba from permissible profit and valid transactions.

Key Terms
الربا (al-ribā): A stipulated surplus devoid of compensation for one of the two contracting parties in an exchange.
فضل (faḍl): The excess or surplus in quantity or value between two exchanged commodities of the same genus.
خال عن عوض (khāl ‘an ‘iwaḍ): Devoid of a countervalue; the surplus is not received in exchange for something else.
شرط (sharṭ): A condition stipulated at the time of contract that the surplus be given to one party.
معاوضة (mu‘āwaḍah): An exchange contract where both parties give and receive something of value, including sale and gift with compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition, which of the following is NOT a required element for a transaction to be riba?
A) The surplus must be of the same genus as the countervalue.
B) The surplus must be stipulated in the contract.
C) The surplus must be given voluntarily after the contract.
D) The transaction must be an exchange (mu'awadah).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of riba is precise: it is a stipulated surplus devoid of compensation in an exchange, which excludes ordinary profit and voluntary gifts.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
beginner
8 min
Definition of Riba al-Fadl in Exchange: Surplus without Counter-value
Defines riba al-fadl with conditions and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of riba al-fadl and identify the three essential conditions that must be present for an exchange to fall under this prohibited category.

Core Idea
Riba al-fadl is the extra amount in a homogeneous commodity exchanged by measure or weight, given without compensation, and stipulated for one party.

This lesson defines riba al-fadl (surplus riba) in exchange transactions. The Hanafi school bases this on the principle that any excess in the legal standard between two like items is prohibited if it is without a return and stipulated for a party. We will break down the definition, examine examples of what is and is not riba, and understand why certain transactions are excluded.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four key components of the definition
From the text: (1) surplus (faḍl) of one of two homogeneous items over the other, (2) by the legal standard (mi‘yār shar‘ī: measure or weight), (3) free of any counter-value (khāl ‘an al-‘iwaḍ), and (4) stipulated for one of the two contracting parties (sharṭ li-aḥad al-‘āqidayn).
Memorize these four conditions; they are the litmus test for riba al-faḍl.
2
Understand the legal standard for homogeneity
The text states that the legal standard is either measure (kayl) or weight (wazn). Items sold by length, number, or other measures are not covered. Example: cloth by length does not trigger riba because it is not a standard.
Hanafis restrict riba to items sold by measure or weight, unlike other schools that include edibles or currency.
3
Differentiate between riba and non-riba examples
The text gives examples: surplus of barley over wheat (different genus) is not riba; surplus of cloth length is not riba. The condition of homogeneity means same genus (jins). The surplus must be without any counter-value; if a different genus is given in return, the surplus is not considered riba because it is not 'free of compensation'.
Note the contrast: wheat and barley are not homogeneous, so exchange of unequal amounts is permissible.
4
Recognize the condition of being stipulated for a party
The surplus must be a condition in the contract for one of the parties. If the surplus is stipulated for someone else or arises spontaneously, it does not fall under riba. Also, gifts with a condition of compensation (e.g., service) are treated like sales after possession.
This condition excludes cases where the extra amount is offered voluntarily after the contract.

Source Text

Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage defines riba in exchange (riba al-fadl) as any excess in the legal standard (measure or weight) between two homogeneous items without a corresponding counter-value. The author clarifies conditions: the surplus must be free of any return, and it must be stipulated for one of the parties. Examples exclude items like cloth where standard measure is length. The commentary further explains that the definition applies even to gifts with conditions, as they become like sales after possession. The Hanafi position is that only transactions where one party gains an excess without consideration fall under riba al-fadl.

Key Terms
الفضل (al-faḍl): The surplus or excess of one homogeneous item over another in exchange.
المعيار الشرعي (al-mi‘yār al-shar‘ī): The legal standard for evaluating equivalence: measure (kayl) for measurable items and weight (wazn) for weighable items.
المتجانسين (al-mutajānisayn): Ones of the same genus (jins), e.g., wheat and barley are not homogeneous; but wheat and wheat are.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the surplus of ten cubits of cloth over five cubits of the same cloth not considered riba according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because cloth is not a homogeneous item.
B) Because cloth is sold by length, which is not a legal standard for riba.
C) Because the surplus is with a counter-value.
D) Because the condition is stipulated for someone else.

Key Takeaway
Riba al-fadl is any excess in measure or weight between two homogeneous items without a return, and it remains riba only if the excess is stipulated for one of the parties.

The Effective Cause of Riba: Genus and Measure vs. Food and Currency
Dispute on the effective cause of riba between Hanafi and Shafi'i schools

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi 'illa for riba (al-qudra ma'a al-jins), identify the Shafi'i counter-argument (al-tu'm wa al-thamaniyya), and explain why the Hanafi school relies on the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri.

Core Idea
Riba's cause is genus and standard measure, not edibility—this determines which transactions are prohibited.

The prohibition of riba is settled, but the 'illa (effective cause) that triggers that prohibition is debated between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. This dispute is not merely theoretical; it determines which items fall under the same ruling and thus which exchanges are valid or invalid. In this lesson, we dissect the Hanafi position that the 'illa is the combination of genus (jins) and standard measure (qudra: capacity for measurable items, weight for weighable items), and contrast it with the Shafi'i view that the 'illa is edibility (tu'm) for food and currency status (thamaniya) for money. We will examine the proofs from hadith and the practical consequences of this difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Need for an 'Illa
Riba fadl (excess in exchange) is prohibited. The 'illa is the common attribute that triggers this prohibition in the ribawi items. Without identifying the 'illa, you cannot extend the ruling to new items or understand the scope of prohibition.
The term 'illa is used in usul to mean the attribute upon which the ruling depends.
2
State the Hanafi 'Illa and Its Components
The Hanafi 'illa is 'al-qudra ma'a al-jins' – standard measure (capacity for kayl items, weight for wazn items) together with unity of genus. Both must be present for the prohibition of excess.
Genus unity means the two items must be from the same kind (e.g., wheat with wheat).
3
State the Shafi'i 'Illa and Its Components
According to Imam al-Shafi'i (and Ahmad in one narration), the 'illa is edibility (tu'm) for food items and currency status (thamaniya) for gold and silver. This restricts ribawi items to foodstuffs and money.
The term 'food' is derived from 'taste' (tu'm), and the sigha of derivation indicates causation.
4
Compare the Proofs: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
The Hanafi proof is the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri listing six specific items (gold, silver, wheat, barley, dates, salt) and requiring equality 'like for like' within each genus. The Shafi'i proof is the hadith of Ma'mar ibn Abdillah: 'Food for food like for like.' Hanafis argue their hadith is more explicit and enumerative, while Shafi'is interpret 'food' to cover all edibles.
The Hanafi rebuttal: the word 'food' is general and not exclusive, so the enumerative hadith specifies the cause.
5
Apply the 'Illa to Determine Practical Consequences
Under the Hanafi view, any item measured by capacity or weight, when exchanged within its genus, cannot have a surplus. Under Shafi'i, only foodstuffs and currencies are ribawi; other measured items (e.g., iron, cloth) are not. This affects everyday transactions like exchanging different qualities of rice or metal.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is the scope of prohibited transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And its cause is capacity with genus. (And its cause is capacity with genus: the meaning of capacity is measure in measured items, and weight in weighed items. [1] Commentary: 'And its cause' requires the obligation of equivalence which necessitates riba when it is absent, or it is the cause of the item being riba or the cause of prohibition of surplus, so it is not objected that selling measured or weighed items with their own genus in equal amount is valid despite the existence of the cause: namely capacity and genus. Capacity linguistically: the state of a thing being equal to another without increase or decrease, and legally: equality in the legal standard requiring formal equivalence, which is measure and weight. And the intended meaning of genus is the unity in the two counter-values, so the cause is the combination of the two descriptions according to us. [2] Commentary: 'And according to al-Shafi'i: is it taste...' etc., and Ahmad also holds it in one narration; because Muslim reported from Ma'mar ibn 'Abdillah that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Food for food like for like, and our food that day was barley.' The indication is that food is derived from taste, and when a ruling is linked to a derived noun, the basis of derivation is its cause. And our proof is what Muslim reported from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri (may Allah be pleased with him) who said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Gold for gold, silver for silver, wheat for wheat, barley for barley, dates for dates, salt for salt, like for like, hand to hand. Whoever increases or asks for increase has engaged in riba, the taker and the giver are equal.' The indication is that He obligated equivalence in one genus to complete the benefit...
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage discusses the effective cause ('illa) of riba according to the Hanafi school and contrasts it with the Shafi'i position. The Hanafi school holds that the 'illa is the combination of genus (jins) and standard measure (qudra: capacity for measurable items and weight for weighable items). The Shafi'i school holds that the 'illa for riba in day-to-day items is that it is a food substance (tu'm), and for currency it is the status of being a medium of exchange (thamaniyya). The text then provides a refutation: The Hanafi school uses the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri which lists six specific items and requires equality within each genus. This indicates that the 'illa is genus and the common factor of measure/weight (since all six items are either measured or weighed by custom). The Shafi'i proof from the hadith 'food for food' is answered by noting that the term 'food' is derived from 'taste' but the Hanafi position is based on a more explicit hadith that specifies the cause. The Hanafi ta'lil (legal reasoning) is that the 'illa is the combination of genus and standard measure because these are the common factors among the ribawi items. This dispute has practical consequences: The Hanafi school applies riba rules to all items sold by measure or weight when exchanged for their own kind, while the Shafi'i school restricts it to foodstuffs and money.

Key Terms
القدر (al-qudra): The standard measure (kayl for measurable, wazn for weighable) used to determine equivalence in ribawi items.
الجنس (al-jins): The genus or kind that determines homogeneity between two items in exchange.
الطعم (al-ṭu‘m): The quality of being a food substance; the Shafi'i 'illa for riba in food items.
الثمنية (al-thamaniya): The quality of being a medium of exchange (currency); the Shafi'i 'illa for riba in gold, silver, and money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the complete 'illa of riba for items like barley and dates?
A) They are foodstuffs.
B) They are measured by weight.
C) They are measured by capacity and are of the same genus.
D) They are currencies.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the effective cause of riba is the combination of genus and standard measure, derived from the explicit hadith of the six items; this is broader than the Shafi'i 'illa of edibility and currency.

The Hanafi 'Illa of Riba: Quantity with Genus vs. Taste/Price
Explaining the Hanafi 'illa of riba and refuting the Shafi'i view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi composite 'illa of riba, contrast it with the Shafi'i position, and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to identify which items are ribawi in each school.

Core Idea
The cause of riba is the composite of quantity (weight/volume) and genus – not just taste or price.

This lesson examines the central inter-madhhab dispute on the cause (ʿilla) of riba. The Hanafi school holds that riba attaches to any item measured by volume or weight when exchanged within the same genus. The Shafiʿi school limits riba to foodstuffs (based on taste) and currencies. Understanding this difference is key to determining which transactions fall under ribawi restrictions in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing definitions of the 'illa
State the Hanafi composite 'illa: quantity (kayl/wazn) combined with genus (jins). State the Shafi'i 'illa: taste (tu'm) for foodstuffs and price (thamaniyya) for currencies.
The Hanafi 'illa is composite; the Shafi'i is not composite across all items.
2
Analyze the Hanafi textual evidence
Examine the six-commodities hadith (Muslim) and the 'Ubada/Anas hadith (Daraqutni). The Prophet attached the ruling to measurement/weight and genus, indicating that these are the cause.
The six-commodities hadith explicitly mentions gold, silver, wheat, barley, dates, salt – all are measured/weighed.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i textual evidence
The Shafi'i rely on the hadith 'food for food, like for like' and the derivation of 'food' from 'taste' (tu'm). They argue that when the ruling is attached to a derived noun, the source of derivation is the cause.
The Hanafi response is that the explicit texts override this derivation.
4
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi rebuttal is that the explicit hadith (six commodities) includes non-food items like gold and silver, and the Daraqutni hadith directly attaches the ruling to measurement and genus. The word 'food' in the Shafi'i proof is not absolute and the derivation argument is weaker.
The Hanafi response uses the principle that explicit text takes precedence over implied meaning.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: items that are measured/weighed but not food (e.g., iron, cotton) are ribawi in the Hanafi school but not in the Shafi'i school. Items like fabrics (not measured/weighed) are not ribawi in either school.
This difference affects everyday transactions in markets and finance.

[١] قوله : والجنسية ؛ شرط لعمل العلّة عملها حتى لا تعمل علته، وهي الطعم في المطعومات والثمنيّة في الأثمان عنده إلا عند وجودِ الجنسية، ولا أثر للجنسية بانفرادها عنده ، كذا في «الكفاية) (۲) وفائدة كون الجنسيّة شرطاً وأحد وصفي العلّة عندنا إنما يظهر فيما إذا وجدت الجنسية في غير الأموال الربوية ، هل يحرم النسأ كما لو أسلم ثوباً هرويًا في هروي لا يجوز عندنا ؛ لوجود أحد وصفي العلة، فيثبت الحكم، وذلك حرمة النسأ، وعنده : يجوز؛ لأن الجنسيّة شرط، وإنما تعمل العلة عند وجود شرطها ، لا أنَّ الشرط يعمل شيئاً من العمل عند عدم العلة، كذا في «النهاية».
And its cause is quantity with genus. (And its cause is quantity with genus: what is meant by quantity is volume in things measured by volume, and weight in things weighed. And according to al-Shafi'i: taste in foodstuffs and price in currencies. [1] His statement: and its cause; for the obligation of equivalence, the lack of which necessitates riba, or the cause of wealth being riba or the cause of the prohibition of excess. So it is not objected that selling measured/weighed items of the same genus in equal amounts is valid despite the existence of the cause, namely quantity and genus. This is as in Sharh al-Birjandi. And 'illa in language means disease. In the terminology of usul: that to which the establishment of a ruling is attributed without an intermediary. Thus condition (shart) is excluded because the ruling is not attributed to its establishment; likewise cause (sabab), sign (alama), and cause of the cause (illat al-illa) because they are with an intermediary, as in al-Minah. Quantity with genus: This is more comprehensive than the statement of some: it is volume with genus or weight with genus; because it encompasses both. Quantity linguistically: a thing being equal to another without increase or decrease; legally: equality in the legal standard that necessitates formal equivalence, which is volume and weight. (3) [2] His statement: And according to al-Shafi'i: taste... etc. And Ahmad followed him in one narration; due to what Muslim narrated from the hadith of Ma'mar ibn Abdillah that the Prophet (saw) said: 'Food for food, like for like, and our food at that time was barley.' And for us is what Muslim narrated from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri (ra) that the Messenger of Allah (saw) said: 'Gold for gold, silver for silver, wheat for wheat, barley for barley, dates for dates, salt for salt, like for like, hand to hand; so whoever increases or seeks increase has committed riba, the taker and the giver are equal.' The indication is that he obligated equivalence in the same genus to complete the benefit for both contracting parties, because if one countervalue were less than the other, benefit would be complete for one party only. Equivalence in form is quantity, and in meaning is genus. And what is narrated from 'Ubada and Anas: that the Prophet (saw) said: 'What is weighed, like for like, if it is one type; and what is measured is likewise; so if the types differ, there is no harm.' Narrated by al-Daraqutni. Thus he attached the ruling to genus and quantity. This is an explicit text that they are the cause of the ruling, as we have understood earlier that attaching the ruling to a derived noun indicates that the source of derivation is a cause for that ruling. Thus its meaning is: measured/weighed items are like for like due to measurement/weight together with genus. Also, the Messenger of Allah (saw) conditioned equivalence by his saying: like for like, and equivalence is by weight or volume, not otherwise. It is therefore known that what is not measured or weighed is not among ribawi properties. Hence they said: Riba occurs in what is not subject to the legal standard like a grain of wheat or barley, and like a grain of gold or silver. Thus declared by al-Allama al-'Ayni and other jurists and eminent scholars, may Allah include them in the Abode of Peace and perpetuate their effusions until the Day of Judgment. [1] His statement: And genus (jinsiyya) is a condition for the cause to operate; so that his cause, namely taste in foodstuffs and price in currencies, does not operate except when genus exists. And genus alone has no effect according to him, as in al-Kifaya. The benefit of genus being a condition and one of the two descriptors of the cause according to us appears when genus exists in non-ribawi properties: Is deferred sale (nasa') prohibited? For example, if one delivers a Herawi garment for a Herawi garment, it is not permissible according to us because one of the two descriptors of the cause exists, so the ruling (prohibition of nasa') is established. According to him, it is permissible because genus is a condition, and the cause only operates when its condition exists; the condition does not operate at all in the absence of the cause, as in al-Nihaya.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute on the effective cause ('illa) of riba in the chapter of sales. The commentator explains the Hanafi position and refutes the Shafi'i view. The text begins by stating the Hanafi 'illa: 'al-qadr ma'a al-jins' (quantity with genus). Quantity here means volume for items measured by volume and weight for items weighed. This is contrasted with al-Shafi'i's 'illa: taste (tu'm) for foodstuffs and price (thamaniyya) for currencies. The commentator then clarifies the terminology: 'illa in usul al-fiqh is that to which the ruling is directly attributed, excluding conditions, causes, signs, and causes of causes. The Hanafi 'illa is composite; it is the combination of quantity (volume/weight) and genus (jins). The author then provides evidence from ahadith. The Shafi'i rely on the hadith 'food for food, like for like' arguing that 'food' derives from 'taste', so taste is the cause. The Hanafi respond with the famous hadith of six commodities (gold, silver, wheat, barley, dates, salt) which explicitly mentions both species and measurement. Additionally, the hadith of 'Ubada and Anas reported by al-Daraqutni explicitly conditions the ruling on genus and measurement. The commentator argues that attaching a ruling to a derived noun indicates causality in the source of derivation; here the nouns 'measured' and 'weighed' indicate that the cause is the act of measurement/weighing along with genus. He concludes that only items that are measured or weighed can be ribawi; things like single grains or gold/silver as raw material (not coined) are also subject to the same standard. Finally, the text discusses genus as a condition (shart) for the cause. According to the Hanafis, genus is one of the two components of the 'illa; its presence without quantity (e.g., in non-ribawi items like cloth) does not trigger the full riba ruling, but it does affect the prohibition of deferred sale (nasa') because one of the two descriptors exists. In contrast, al-Shafi'i treats genus only as a condition; if the cause (taste/price) is absent, genus alone has no effect. This is illustrated with the example of exchanging a Herawi garment for a Herawi garment on credit: Hanafis say it is not permissible because quantity is absent but genus exists (so one descriptor suffices for nasa'); Shafi'is allow it because taste/price is absent and genus alone is insufficient.

Key Terms
علة ('illa): In usul al-fiqh, the attribute to which the ruling is directly attributed; here the composite of quantity and genus for riba.
القدر (al-qadr): Quantity: volume (kayl) for items measured by volume, weight (wazn) for items weighed.
الجنس (al-jins): Genus: the unity of type (jins) between the two countervalues in a transaction.
المماثلة (al-mumathala): Equivalence: equality in quantity and genus in ribawi exchanges.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Genus alone
D) Quantity (volume/weight) combined with genus

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi 'illa of riba is the composite of quantity (volume/weight) and genus, derived from explicit hadith, making any measured/weighed item of the same genus ribawi.

Genus as Condition for Riba: The Shart Distinction
Expansion probe: clarifying the role of genus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the cause (ʿilla) from the condition (shart) for riba according to the Hanafī school and apply this distinction to determine the ruling on deferred sale of same-genus non-ribawī items.

Core Idea
Genus is a condition for riba’s cause, not the cause itself; without it, the cause has no effect.

This passage from al-ʿInāya (a commentary on al-Wiqāya) explicates the exact role of genus (jinsiyya) in the prohibition of riba. The author asserts that genus is a condition (shart) for the cause of riba to operate, not the cause itself. This seemingly subtle distinction leads to a concrete difference of opinion within the Hanafī school: does the mere presence of the same genus (even in non-ribawī items) trigger the prohibition of deferred sale? Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying riba rules in real transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cause of riba according to Abu Hanifa
Determine what the Hanafī school identifies as the primary cause (ʿilla) of riba in different categories. According to the passage, the cause is taʿm (taste/edibility) in foodstuffs and thamaniyya (being a medium of exchange) in currencies.
This is a foundational step: without the cause, no prohibition is triggered.
2
Identify the role of genus (jinsiyya)
Recognize that genus is not part of the cause but a condition (shart) for the cause to operate. The cause alone does not create prohibition unless the exchanged items share the same genus.
For example, selling wheat for barley (different genera) deferred is permissible because the condition (same genus) is absent.
3
Apply the principle to a specific case
Consider the case: exchanging one Herawi garment for another Herawi garment (same genus, but garments are not ribawī items) with deferred payment. According to the preferred Hanafī view, this is prohibited because one of the two descriptions of the cause (jinsiyya) exists. According to another view (attributed to some scholars), it is permissible because genus alone is not the cause.
This case illustrates the practical consequence (thamara al-khilāf) of the condition vs. cause distinction.
4
State the muftā bihī position
The relied-upon position is that deferred sale of same-genus items is prohibited even if the items are not ribawī (like cloth), as long as the genus is the same. This is the view adopted in the later literature (e.g., al-Nihāya).
Al-Nihāya is the source of the tarjīḥ.
5
Summarize the condition-cause framework
Conclude that in the Hanafī school, the cause of riba (taʿm/ thamaniyya) requires the condition of same genus to be effective. Equality (musāwāt) in quantity removes the prohibition in spot exchanges, but deferred payment remains prohibited when genus is present.
This completes the analytical framework for understanding riba al-faḍl and riba al-nasīʾa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجنسيّة شرط ، والمساواتُ مخلص، والأصل الحرمة.
[قوله : والجنسية ؛ شرط لعمل العلّة عملها حتى لا تعمل علته، وهي الطعم في المطعومات والثمنيّة في الأثمان عنده إلا عند وجودِ الجنسية، ولا أثر للجنسية بانفرادها عنده ، كذا في «الكفاية) (۲) وفائدة كون الجنسيّة شرطاً وأحد وصفي العلّة عندنا إنما يظهر فيما إذا وجدت الجنسية في غير الأموال الربوية ، هل يحرم النسأ كما لو أسلم ثوباً هرويًا في هروي لا يجوز عندنا ؛ لوجود أحد وصفي العلة، فيثبت الحكم، وذلك حرمة النسأ، وعنده : يجوز؛ لأن الجنسيّة شرط، وإنما تعمل العلة عند وجود شرطها ، لا أنَّ الشرط يعمل شيئاً من العمل عند عدم العلة، كذا في «النهاية».
And genus is a condition, and equality is a way out, and the original ruling is prohibition. [His saying: and genus; is a condition for the cause to work its effect, so that his cause does not work, and it is taste in foodstuffs and money-ness in currencies according to him except when genus exists, and genus alone has no effect according to him. This is in al-Kifayah. And the benefit of genus being a condition and one of the two descriptions of the cause according to us appears when genus is found in non-ribawi properties: is deferred sale prohibited, as if one gives a Herawi garment for a Herawi garment? It is not permissible according to us because one of the two descriptions of the cause exists, so the ruling is established, which is prohibition of deferred sale; and according to him it is permissible because genus is a condition, and the cause only works when its condition exists, not that the condition does any work when the cause is absent. This is in al-Nihayah.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage clarifies the role of genus (jinsiyyah) in riba. The author states that genus is a condition (shart) for the cause (illah) of riba to operate. The cause itself, according to Abu Hanifa, is taste (ta'm) in foodstuffs and being a medium of exchange (thamaniyyah) in currencies. However, these causes only trigger the prohibition of riba when the two exchanged items share the same genus. Without the condition of genus, the cause has no effect. The passage then highlights a practical difference: if two non-ribawi items of the same genus are exchanged with deferred payment (nasi'ah), according to our Hanafi position it is prohibited because one of the two descriptions of the cause (genus) exists, leading to the prohibition of deferred riba. But according to some (possibly Imam Muhammad or others), it is permissible because genus alone is not the cause but only a condition, and without the actual cause (taste or thamaniyyah) the condition does nothing.

Key Terms
الجنسيّة (al-jinsiyyah): The condition that the two exchanged items belong to the same genus (jins) for riba to apply.
المساوات (al-musawat): Equality in measure or weight when exchanging same-genus items, which makes the exchange permissible (mubāḥ).
العلة (al-ʿillah): The cause that triggers the ruling of riba: ta'm (taste/edibility) in foodstuffs and thamaniyyah (medium of exchange) in currencies.
الشرط (al-shart): A condition that must exist for the cause to operate, but does not itself produce the ruling.

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the role of genus (jinsiyyah) in riba?
A) It is the cause (illah) of prohibition.
B) It is a condition (shart) for the cause to operate.
C) It is irrelevant to riba.
D) It is the only factor determining riba.

Key Takeaway
Genus is a condition (shart) for the cause of riba to operate; equality (musāwāt) removes prohibition in spot exchanges, but deferment (nasīʾa) with same genus triggers prohibition even in non-ribawī items.

The Hanafi Cause of Riba in Commodities: Kayl and Wazn
States ruling, examples, and inter-madhhab dispute on cause of riba

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the cause of riba in commodities, explain the opposing Shafi'i and Maliki positions, and identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) in rulings on non-food items.

Core Idea
The cause of riba in commodities is being measured or weighed and same genus, not taste or storage.

This passage from the Hanafi tradition tackles a fundamental question in the law of riba: what exactly triggers the prohibition of excess in sales of the same type of commodity? The answer determines whether non-food items like gypsum or iron fall under riba. The Hanafi school takes a broad view, while the Shafi'i and Maliki schools adopt narrower causes. By understanding this dispute, you will grasp the role of legal cause (illah) in shaping fiqh and the practical consequences of each school's methodology.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling and its basis
Identify the ruling (prohibition of excess in sale of same genus measured/weighed items) and the Hanafi cause: kayl (measurement) and wazn (weight) combined with genus (jins).
The ruling is derived from the hadith 'Weigh for weigh if the same type' and the principle that the ruling is attached to the name indicating the cause.
2
Identify the opposing schools and their causes
State that Imam Malik holds the cause to be ta'm (taste) and iddikhar (storage), while Imam al-Shafi'i holds it to be ta'm and thaman (medium of exchange).
These differences arise from different methods of identifying the illah (tahrir al-manat).
3
Follow the opponents' proof chain
Understand why Shafi'i and Maliki schools exclude non-food items like gypsum: because they lack taste and are not stored (Maliki) or not a medium of exchange (Shafi'i). Thus, selling gypsum for gypsum with excess is permissible for them.
Their dalil includes analogies to the six ribawi items mentioned in the hadith.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the Prophet ﷺ conditioned equality (mithlan bi mithlin) in the hadith, indicating that the cause is the measurable/weighable nature and genus, not taste or storage. Also, the ruling is attached to the name 'kayli' and 'wazni' which imply the cause.
For Hanafis, the hadith overrides analogical reasoning (qiyas) that might limit the cause.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis prohibit selling non-food measured/weighed items (e.g., gypsum, iron) of same genus with excess; Shafi'is and Malikis permit such sales.
This shows how a difference in illah leads to different halal/haram boundaries in everyday transactions.

فحرم بيع الكيلي والوزني بجنسه متفاضلا ولو غير مطعوم: كالجص، والحديد، وحل متماثلاً ، وبلا معيار كحفنة بحفنتين، وبيضة ببيضتين، وتمرة بتمرتين وعند مالك) : علته الطعم والادخار.
[۲] قوله : فحرم ; تفريع على كون القدر والجنس علة تحريم الفضل. قوله : متفاضلاً، فوجه حرمة البيع في صورة التفاضل وجود الربا في ذلك ، وكذا يحرم ذلك البيع نسيئة لشبه الفضل، إذ النقد خير من النسا.
[٤] قوله : على ما ذكرنا ; فيجوز ذلك البيع عند الشافعي له لعدم علة الربا ، وهي الطعم والثمنية ، وعند مالك الله : لعدم الطعم والادخار.
[ ] قوله : كحفنة...الخ ; مثالُ الأشياء التي يجوز بيعها متماثلاً ، ووجه الجواز فيها لانتفاء جريان الكيل، وما دون نصف صاع ; لأنه لا تقدير للشرع بما دونه ، ولما كان الحفنة والحفنتين لا تدخل تحت المعيار الشرعي كان مضموناً بالقيمة عند الإتلاف، وأمّا إذا كان أحد البدلين يبلغ حد نصف الصَّاعِ أو أكثر، والآخر لم يبلغه، فلا يجوز، كذا في «العناية».
So the sale of the measured and weighed is prohibited with its genus with excess, even if not food: like gypsum and iron. And it is permissible with equality. And without [standard] measure, like a handful for two handfuls, and an egg for two eggs, and a date for two dates. And according to Malik, its cause is taste and storage.
[His saying: so it is prohibited; is a branching upon the fact that quantity and genus are the cause of prohibition of excess. His saying: with excess; so the aspect of prohibition of sale in the case of excess is the existence of riba in that, and similarly that sale is prohibited on credit due to resemblance to excess, since cash is better than credit.
[His saying: upon what we mentioned; so that sale is permissible according to al-Shafi'i due to the absence of the cause of riba, which is taste and money-ness, and according to Malik due to the absence of taste and storage.
[His saying: like a handful...etc.; is an example of things that are permissible to sell with equality, and the aspect of permissibility in them is due to the non-application of measurement, and that which is less than half a sa' (because there is no legal estimation for what is less than that), and since a handful and two handfuls do not fall under the legal standard, they are guaranteed by value when destroyed. But if one of the two countervalues reaches the limit of half a sa' or more and the other does not reach it, it is not permissible. This is in al-'Inayah.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage delivers the core ruling on riba in commodities that are measured by volume (kayl) or weight (wazn). When selling such items of the same genus, any excess (tafadul) is prohibited, even if the item is not food (e.g., gypsum, iron). Spot sale with exact equality (tamathul) is permissible. Items that do not have a standard legal measure (e.g., handfuls, eggs, dates) are not subject to riba rules; their sale with excess is permissible. The passage then presents the inter-madhhab dispute: Imam Malik's cause for riba is taste and storage (iddikhar), while Imam Shafi'i's cause is taste and being a medium of exchange (thaman). Thus, according to Shafi'i, selling non-food items like gypsum for excess is permissible because the cause is absent. According to Malik, such items are also permissible because they are not edible or storable. The commentary elaborates that the prohibition of selling on credit (nasi’ah) in these cases is similar to the prohibition of excess because cash is superior to credit. Finally, the commentary notes that items less than half a sa' are not subject to legal measure; hence, selling a handful for two handfuls is permissible. However, if one countervalue reaches half a sa' or more and the other less, the sale is not permissible because the minimum measure for standard measurement is half a sa'.

Key Terms
الكَيْلِي (al-kayli): Items that are measured by volume (like wheat, barley, dates).
الوَزْنِي (al-wazni): Items that are weighed (like iron, gold, silver).
المِعْيَار الشَّرْعِي (al-mi'yar al-shar'i): The legal standard measure recognized in fiqh, such as the sa' for volume and raṭl for weight.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is selling gypsum (a non-food item) of the same genus with excess prohibited?
A) Because gypsum is edible and storable.
B) Because gypsum is a medium of exchange.
C) Because gypsum is measured or weighed, and the genus condition is met.
D) Because gypsum is considered a luxury.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits any excess in the sale of same-genus items that are measured or weighed, even if not food, because the cause is measurement/weight and genus, not taste or storage.

Riba in Kayli and Wazni Items: The Cause and Exception for Non-Standard Measures
Explains prohibition of riba in measured/weighed items and permissibility of unequal sale for non-standard measures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on exchanging kayli/wazni items of the same kind, and explain why unequal exchange of a handful of dates for two handfuls is permissible.

Core Idea
The effective cause (illa) of riba is capacity and weight; items outside these measures are not subject to riba.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains a key Hanafi principle in sales: riba (usury) is prohibited only in items that have a standard legal measure—either by volume (kayl) or by weight (wazn). When such items of the same kind are exchanged, they must be equal in amount. However, for items that do not fall under a standard measure—like a handful of dates or an egg—the Hanafi school permits unequal exchange. This ruling hinges on the definition of the effective cause (illa) for riba and leads to a significant difference with the Shafi'i school. Let's explore the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling
First, note the core ruling: selling kayli or wazni items of the same kind in unequal amounts is prohibited (haram). Selling them equally is permissible. For items without a standard measure, unequal sale is permissible.
2
Understand the effective cause (illa)
The Hanafi school holds that the illa for riba in sales is that the item is measured by volume or weight according to Shariah standards. This is based on the principle that riba attaches to items like wheat, barley, dates, gold, silver, etc., which were traditionally measured or weighed.
Other schools have different illas: Shafi'i says edibility and monetary value; Maliki says edibility and storability.
3
Apply the illa to the exception
Items that do not enter into the standard measure—such as a handful (less than half a sa') or countable items like eggs—fall outside the scope of riba. The original principle of permissibility (al-asl al-hill) applies. Thus, selling a handful of dates for two handfuls is allowed.
If one item reaches half a sa' or more while the other does not, the sale becomes impermissible because it then falls under the standard measure.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i, based on his illa of edibility and monetary value, prohibits unequal exchange of any food item, including handfuls. The Hanafi response clarifies that the hadith ' لا تبيعوا الطعام بالطعام إلا سواء بسواء ' is specific to food that enters the standard measure, not all food.
This difference is a practical example of ikhtilaf with a clear thamarah (outcome).

وعند الشَّافِعِي لا يحل بيع المطعومات حفنة بحفنتين بناء على ما ذكرنا من العلة، وبناءً على أن الأصل عندنا الحل، وعنده الحرمة، فعندنا ما يدخلُ في الكيل يثبت فيه الحرمة ، وما لا يدخلُ فيه يَبْقَى على أصله، وهو الحل، وعند الشَّافِعِي الأصل هو الحرمة، والمساواة مخلص فيما لا يَدْخُلُ في المسوى الشرعي، وهو الكيلُ يبقى على الأصل، وهو الحرمة ، وإنما جعل الحرمة أصلاً ؛ لقوله :
لا تبيعوا الطَّعامَ بالطَّعام إلا سواء بسواء فما لا يكون مساوياً كان حراماً. قلنا : المعنى لا تبيعوا الطعام الذي يدخلُ في المسوى الشرعي إلا سواء بسواء والحاصل أنَّ ما لا يدخل تحت المعيار إما لقلة كالحفنة والحفنتين، والتمرة والتمرتين، وإما لكونه عددياً لا يباع بالمعيار الشرعي كالبيضة والبيضتين، والجوزة والجوزتين يحل البيع متفاضلاً ؛ لعدم جريان القدر والمعيار، فلا يوجد المساواة، فلم يتميّز الفضلُ وبقي على الأصل، وهو الحل عندنا.

It is prohibited to sell by volume or weight items of the same kind in unequal amounts, even if not edible, like plaster and iron. And it is permissible to sell them equally. And without a standard measure, like a handful for two handfuls, an egg for two eggs, a date for two dates. And according to Malik, its cause is taste and storability. [2] (He said: Prohibited is the sale of kayli and wazni of its kind in unequal amounts, even if not edible, like plaster and iron) Plaster is among measured items and iron among weighed items, and regarding them there is disagreement with Shafi'i and Malik, based on what we mentioned of the cause. (2) (And permissible equally) meaning the sale of the mentioned items. (And without a standard measure) meaning the sale is permissible differentially in what does not enter the standard measure, (like a handful for two handfuls, an egg for two eggs, a date for two dates). [1 His saying: its cause is taste and storability; because he specified by mention every nutritious and storable item. [2] His saying: prohibited; a branch on the capacity and genus being the cause of prohibition of excess. His saying: differentially; so the aspect of prohibition of sale in the case of differential is the presence of riba therein, and likewise that sale is prohibited on credit due to similarity to excess, since cash is better than credit. [4] His saying: based on what we mentioned; so that sale is permissible according to Shafi'i due to the absence of the cause of riba, which is taste and monetary value, and according to Malik due to the absence of taste and storability. [ ] His saying: like a handful...etc.; example of things whose sale is permissible equally, and the aspect of permissibility therein is due to the absence of measurement by volume, and what is less than half a sa' because there is no estimation by shariah for what is less than that, and since the handful and two handfuls do not enter under the standard measure, it is guaranteed by value in case of destruction, but if one of the two items reaches the limit of half a sa' or more and the other does not reach it, it is not permissible. And according to Shafi'i, the sale of foodstuffs in handful for two handfuls is not permissible, based on what we mentioned of the cause, and based on the fact that with us the original ruling is permissibility, while with him it is prohibition. So with us, what enters into measurement by volume has prohibition established, and what does not enter remains on its original ruling, which is permissibility. And according to Shafi'i, the original ruling is prohibition, and equality is a means of release (from prohibition) for what does not enter into the equalizing standard, which is measurement by volume; it remains on the original ruling, which is prohibition. And he made prohibition the original ruling based on his saying: 'Do not sell food for food except like for like.' For what is not equal is unlawful. We said: the meaning is 'Do not sell food that enters into the equalizing standard except like for like.' The upshot is that what does not enter under the standard, either due to small quantity like a handful and two handfuls, a date and two dates, or because it is countable and not sold by shariah standard like an egg and two eggs, a walnut and two walnuts, the sale is permissible differentially due to the absence of capacity and standard measure, so equality is not present, thus the excess is not distinguishable, so it remains on the original ruling, which is permissibility with us. Contrary to Shafi'i, due to the presence of the cause of prohibition, which is taste, without the means of release, which is equality, so it is prohibited, because the original ruling with him is prohibition.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is an intra- and inter-madhhab discussion on the effective cause (illa) for riba in sales. The author begins by stating the Hanafi ruling: it is prohibited (haram) to sell by volume (kayli) or by weight (wazni) items of the same genus in unequal amounts, even if the items are not edible, such as plaster (measured) and iron (weighed). The prohibition applies whether the excess is obvious (like more weight) or deferred (credit sale is also prohibited because it resembles riba al-fadl). The sale is permissible only when the amounts are equal. For items that do not have a standard legal measure – like a handful of dates for two handfuls, or an egg for two eggs – the Hanafi school permits unequal sale. This is because the effective cause (illa) for riba is capacity (kayl) and weight (wazn). Items that are not subject to these measures (e.g., less than half a saʿ or counted items) fall outside the scope of riba. The commentary explains that a handful is less than half a saʿ, and the law does not prescribe a standard for such small amounts; therefore, they are not subject to the prohibition. However, if one of the items reaches half a saʿ or more while the other is less, the sale becomes impermissible because it then falls under the standard measure. The author then contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Imam al-Shafiʿi and Imam Malik. For Shafiʿi, the illa for riba in food is edibility (ṭuʿm) and monetary value (thamanīyah); for Malik, it is edibility (ṭuʿm) and storability (iddikhār). Under these definitions, the rules differ. The core dispute is presented: according to Shafiʿi, even a handful of food for two handfuls is prohibited because the illa (edibility) is present, and the hadith 'لا تبيعوا الطعام بالطعام إلا سواء بسواء' (Do not sell food for food except equal) is general. The Hanafi response clarifies that the hadith is specific to food that enters into the standard measure (al-musāwī al-sharʿī). Items that do not enter the standard measure remain on the original principle of permissibility (al-aṣl al-ḥill). Thus, the Hanafi school permits unequal sale of handfuls, eggs, and similar items, while Shafiʿi prohibits them.

Key Terms
كَيْلِيّ (kayli): Items measured by volume according to shariah standard, such as wheat, barley, dates.
وَزْنِيّ (wazni): Items weighed according to shariah standard, such as gold, silver, iron.
حَفْنَة (hafna): A handful; an amount less than half a sa' that is not subject to standard measure.
عِلَّة (illa): The effective cause for the prohibition of riba: for Hanafi, it is capacity (kayl) and weight (wazn) plus same genus; for Shafi'i, it is edibility and monetary value; for Malik, it is edibility and storability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because dates are not considered food.
B) Because a handful is not subject to standard measure (kayl), so the cause of riba is absent.
C) Because the Hadith does not mention handfuls.
D) Because the buyer and seller agreed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the prohibition of riba in sales depends on the item being subject to standard volume or weight; items outside these measures may be exchanged unequally.

Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Selling Food Unequally: The Riba of Small Quantities
Inter-madhhab dispute on riba in food sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits selling a handful of food for two handfuls and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on the hadith.

Core Idea
Small quantities not subject to measure are not riba; the default remains permissibility.

This passage presents a detailed inter-madhhab dispute about selling food items in unequal quantities when the items are not measured by standard legal measures (kayl or wazn). The Hanafi school holds that such sales are permissible, while Imam al-Shafiʿi considers them prohibited. The dispute hinges on the interpretation of the hadith 'Do not sell food for food except like for like' and on the fundamental legal maxim regarding default rulings in transactions. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi approach to riba and the role of custom and measurement in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that Hanafis allow selling small quantities (like a handful) of the same food type in unequal amounts hand-to-hand because they are not subject to legal measurement.
The default is permissibility (ibaha).
2
Identify the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i prohibits this sale because the hadith's general prohibition applies to all food, and equality is the only deliverance.
The default for Shafi'i is prohibition.
3
Analyze the shared dalil
Both schools cite the same hadith: 'لا تبيعوا الطعام بالطعام إلا سواء بسواء'. The disagreement lies in its interpretation: does 'food' include all types, or only what is customarily measured?
The hadith is zanni in thubut and dalala.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi rebuttal
Hanafis argue that the hadith is restricted by 'urf (custom) to food that is measured by kayl/wazn. They use an analogy: the command 'Do not kill animals except with a knife' does not apply to lice or fleas.
This is a form of takhsis al-amm bi al-urf.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: A Hanafi may sell a handful of wheat for two handfuls of wheat hand-to-hand; a Shafi'i may not. The difference is real in daily transactions.
This highlights how usul differences affect fiqh.
