وجاز بيع الفلس بالفلسين بأعيانهما، واللحم بالحيوان. (وجاز بيع الفلس بالفلسين بأعيانهما: خلافاً لمحمد، له أنَّ الفلوس أثمان فلا تتعين بالتعيين فصار كما إذا كان بغير أعيانهما، وكبيع الدرهم بالدرهمين. ولهما: أن ثمنيتها بالاصطلاح، واصطلاح الغير لا يكون حجة على المتعاقدين، وهما أبطلا ثمنيَّتها؛ لأنهما قصدا تصحيح العقد، ولا وجه له إلا بتعينها وخروجها عن الثمنية، لأنها إذا خرجت من الثمنية تكون أعيانها مطلوبة لا ماليتها، فيمكن أن يُعطي فلسين ويأخذ فلساً طلبا لصورته. واللحم بالحيوان: خلافا لمحمد فإنَّ عنده إذا بيع الحيوان بلحم حيوان من جنسه لا يجوز البيع إلا إذا كان اللحم أكثر من لحم ذلك الحيوان. [1] قوله: له: إن...الخ؛ حاصلهُ أنَّ الفلوس أثمان ثمنيّتها ثبتت باتفاقِ النَّاسِ كلّهم فلا تتعين بالتعين، ألا ترى أنه إذا اشترى ثوباً بفلوس معينة فهلكت قبل التسليم لا يبطل العقد، كما إذا اشترى بدراهم معينة أو دنانير معينة فهلكت. ولما لم تتعين الفلوس فصار هذا البيع كما إذا باع فلساً بفلسين غير معين، أو درهماً بدرهمين، حيث لا يجوز البيعُ في هاتين الصورتين اتفاقاً؛ لأنه يؤدّي إلى الربا فكذا ينبغي أن لا يجوز هاهنا. And it is permissible to sell a fils for two fils with their specific identities, and meat for an animal. (And it is permissible to sell a fils for two fils with their specific identities: contrary to Muhammad, for him that fils are prices (currency), so they are not specified by specification, so it becomes as if it were without their identities, and like selling a dirham for two dirhams. And for the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): that their being prices is by convention, and the convention of others is not a proof against the two contracting parties, and they both nullify their price-ness; because they intend to validate the contract, and there is no way for that except by their specification and exiting from price-ness, because when they exit from price-ness, their specific physical forms are sought, not their monetary value, so it is possible to give two fils and take one fils seeking its form. And meat for an animal: contrary to Muhammad, for according to him, when an animal is sold for meat of an animal of its kind, the sale is not permissible unless the meat is more than the meat of that animal. [1] He said: 'For him: that... etc.'; its summary: that fils are prices, their price-ness is established by the agreement of all people, so they are not specified by specification. Do you not see that if one buys a garment with specific fils and they perish before delivery, the contract is not void, as if one buys with specific dirhams or dinars and they perish. And since fils are not specified, this sale becomes like selling one fils for two fils unspecified, or one dirham for two dirhams, where the sale is not permissible in these two cases by agreement; because it leads to riba, so likewise it should not be permissible here. [2] He said: 'For the two: etc.'; its summary: that fils are not prices by inherent nature, rather the origin is that they are commodities; because they are made of copper, and copper is not price, and price-ness in them is only obtained by the convention of people, and the two contractors have not agreed upon it, so they nullify their price-ness intentionally to validate the contract, for there is no way for validation except that they be specified and exit from price-ness, and the convention of others is not a proof against them, because there is no authority of others over them, so they have the right to revoke that convention by another convention. And when they exit from price-ness, they return to being commodities as they were, and their specific physical forms are sought, not their monetary value, for their monetary value is only by virtue of price-ness and it is removed from them by their convention, so it is possible to give two fils and take one fils seeking its form, not by virtue of its monetary value. [3] He said: 'Of its kind'; and as for if they are different, such as selling cow meat for a sheep, for example
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of selling a specific fils (copper coin) for two specific fils, and selling meat for an animal of the same kind. The ruling in the matn states it is permissible. Imam Muhammad disagrees, arguing that fils are inherently prices (أثمان) like dirhams and dinars, so they cannot be specified; thus selling specific fils for two specific fils is like selling unspecified fils, which leads to riba. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf respond that fils are not prices by nature but by convention (اصطلاح). The contracting parties can nullify that convention and treat the fils as specific commodities (مثمن). Since they intend to validate the contract, they treat the fils as specific goods, making the transaction a permissible exchange of specific items for their physical forms, not their monetary value. Similarly, selling meat for an animal of the same kind: Muhammad requires the meat to be more than the animal's meat to avoid riba, implying the general permissibility is restricted. The commentary explains the reasoning of both sides: Muhammad relies on the universal agreement on fils as prices; the other view emphasizes the contractual freedom to redefine the status of fils.

Key Terms
فلس (fils): A copper coin used as currency by convention; plural فلوس
ثمن (thaman): Price or currency; something that serves as a medium of exchange and measure of value
مثمن (muthamman): Commodity; an object valued for its utility, not as currency
اصطلاح (iṣṭilāḥ): Convention or agreement among people regarding the usage of something

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Muhammad prohibit selling a specific fils for two specific fils?
A) Because fils are made of copper and copper is ribawi
B) Because fils are universally considered prices (ثمن) and cannot be specified, so the transaction resembles riba
C) Because the buyer might not take possession immediately
D) Because the seller must give more than two fils

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows parties to treat fils as commodities, making a 1:2 exchange of specific fils valid without riba.

Selling One Fals for Two Fulus by Specification
Commentary on a disputed ribā ruling: fals for two fulus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on exchanging one fals for two fulus by specifying the coins and evaluate the opposing view of Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
Fulus can exit currency status by party agreement, allowing unequal exchange as a barter of commodities.

Copper coins (fulus) are currency by convention, not by nature. When parties specify the exact coins in a contract, they can nullify that convention. This lesson examines the dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (permissible) and Muhammad al-Shaybani (forbidden) regarding exchanging one fals for two fulus by specifying the coins.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the exact transaction? Selling one fals for two fulus by specifying the specific coins (bi-a'yānihimā). The Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) permits it. Muhammad forbids it.
The specification is key; without specification, it is ribā.
2
Understand Muhammad's proof
Muhammad argues that fulus are universally recognized as currency (thaman) by agreement of all people. Therefore, specifying them does not change their currency status, just like dirhams. The sale would be like selling one dirham for two dirhams, which is ribā.
He analogizes to dirhams: if you buy a garment with specified dirhams and they perish, the contract remains valid, proving they are still currency.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue that fulus are not inherently currency; their currency status is based on local convention (iṣṭilāḥ). The two parties have the right to nullify that convention between themselves, making the coins mere commodities. By specifying them, they exit currency status, so exchanging two for one is a valid barter.
The key is that the parties intended to validate the contract by specifying the coins, which removes ribā.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical consequence? If permissible, you can give two fulus and take one fals by specifying the coins. If forbidden, the same transaction is ribā and invalid.
The madhhab follows Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, so the ruling is permissibility.

And it is permissible to sell a fals for two fulus by their specific selves, and meat for an animal. (And it is permissible to sell a fals for two fulus by their specific selves: contrary to Muhammad. He says that fulus are currencies, so they are not determined by specification, so it becomes like if it were not by their specific selves, and like selling a dirham for two dirhams. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): that their being currency is by convention, and the convention of others is not a proof against the two contractors, and they have nullified their being currency; because they intended to validate the contract, and there is no way except by specifying them and removing them from being currency, because when they leave being currency, their specific selves are sought, not their value, so he can give two fulus and take one fals seeking its form.) (And meat for an animal): contrary to Muhammad, for he holds that when an animal is sold for meat of an animal of its kind, the sale is not permissible unless the meat is more than the meat of that animal. Without possession, it is permissible according to us and according to al-Shafi'i. [1] His statement: 'He says...' etc.; its summary is that fulus are currencies; their being currency is established by the agreement of all people, so they are not determined by specification. Do you not see that if one buys a garment with specified fulus and they perish before delivery, the contract is not void, just as if one buys with specified dirhams or specified dinars and they perish. And since fulus are not determined, this sale becomes like selling a fals for two unspecified fulus, or a dirham for two dirhams, where the sale is not permissible in these two images unanimously; because it leads to riba. So likewise it should not be permissible here. [2] His statement: 'For them...' etc.; its summary is that fulus are not currencies by nature; rather, the original principle is that they are commodities because they are made of copper, which is not a currency. Their being currency only occurs by people's convention, and the two contractors have not agreed upon it, so they nullify their being currency intentionally to validate the contract. There is no way for validation except that they be specific and leave currency. The convention of others is not a proof against them, since others have no authority over them; so they have the right to revoke that convention with another convention. And when they leave currency, they return to being commodities as they were, and their specific selves are sought, not their value, for their value only exists by consideration of currency, and that is removed from them by their convention. So he can give two fulus and take one fals seeking its form, not considering its value. [3] His statement: 'Of its kind'; as for when they are different, like selling beef for a sheep, for example. And flour for its kind by measure, and fresh dates for fresh dates and for dried dates. Cream of the End for the Support of Care [2] So that the excess is in exchange for the saqt, and according to them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) it is permissible absolutely, because it is a sale of a weighed item for a non-weighed item. (And flour for its kind by measure, and fresh dates for fresh dates and for dried dates): this is according to Abu Hanifa, so it is permissible by agreement without considering scarcity or abundance as they have stated. [1] His statement: 'So that...' etc.; meaning so that the meat is in exchange for the meat in the animal, and the remainder is in exchange for the saqt, which is other than meat like skin and so on. Because if the meat is not more but equal or less, then riba would be established due to the increase of saqt if the meat equals the meat in the animal, or due to the increase of meat if the meat is more than the meat in the animal. And 'saqt' (with fath of the letter) is 'any thing' (in Persian). So in 'Muntaha al-Arab', and intended here is everything to which the name 'meat' is not applied, like spleen, intestines, and others. [2) His statement: 'Because it is a sale...' etc.; because the animal is not usually weighed, and its weight cannot be known by weighing; because it may lighten itself by its firmness, so it is not known whether it lightened itself or became heavier, and it may become heavy due to relaxation of its joints. The dead are heavier than the living due to relaxation of joints, and women are heavier than men due to their relaxation. So in 'al-Kifaya' and others. And according to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad, it is not permissible, and it is not permissible on credit by consensus. And in 'Sharh al-Tahawi': If the sheep was slaughtered but not skinned, and he bought it with meat of the sheep, the answer according to all of them is as Muhammad said, and he meant by 'not skinned' not separated from the saqt. And if one bought a live sheep for a slaughtered sheep, it is permissible according to all of them, mentioned by al-Allama al-Ayni in 'Sharh al-Kanz'. [3) His statement: 'And flour for its kind': similar by measure, not unequal, due to unity of name, form, and meaning, and thereby homogeneity is established from all aspects. So the possibility of inequality is not considered as in wheat for wheat. And Ibn al-Fadl restricted it to when they are pressed, otherwise it is not permissible contrary to al-Shafi'i; due to lack of...
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqaya. It discusses three distinct rulings within the chapter of ribā al-faḍl. The first ruling concerns exchanging one fals (copper coin) for two fulus by specifying the actual coins. The Hanafi position (Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf) permits it, while Muḥammad al-Shaybānī forbids it. The proof for Muḥammad is that fulus function as currency (thaman) by universal convention, so specifying them does not remove their currency status; thus the exchange must be equal, otherwise it leads to ribā. He analogizes to dirhams, which if specified and then perish before delivery do not void the contract, proving they remain currency. The Hanafi response is that fulus are not inherently currency; their thumaniyya is established by convention (iṣṭilāḥ). The two parties have the right to nullify that convention between themselves, making the coins mere commodities (aʿyān). By specifying them, they exit currency status, so exchanging two for one is permissible as a barter of specific items. The second ruling is selling a live animal for meat of the same species. Here Muḥammad requires the meat to be more than the meat contained in the live animal to account for non-meat parts (saqṭ). Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf allow it absolutely, arguing it is a sale of a weighed item (meat) for a non-weighed item (live animal), so no ribā. The commentary notes that animals are not usually weighed due to variation, reinforcing the difference in category. The third ruling covers flour for its kind by measure, and fresh dates for fresh dates or dried dates. According to Abū Ḥanīfa, these are permissible by agreement without consideration of equality, because they are homogeneous (mujānis) by name, form, and meaning when measured. The passage also mentions a condition by Ibn al-Faḍl that flour must be pressed, otherwise the ruling differs opposite al-Shāfiʿī.

Key Terms
فلس (fals): A copper coin used as currency by convention, not by intrinsic value (like silver/gold).
ثمنية (thumaniyya): The status of being a currency/price (thaman). In this text, it is acquired by convention for fulus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Ḥanafī madhhab (Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf), why is selling one fals for two fulus by specifying the coins permissible?
A) Because fulus are like dirhams and must be equal.
B) Because the parties have nullified the currency status of the fulus by specifying them, making them commodities.
C) Because the sale includes a condition that the fulus are not weighed.
D) Because Muḥammad al-Shaybānī permitted it.

Key Takeaway
The Ḥanafī madhhab permits exchanging one fals for two fulus when the coins are specified, because the parties can nullify the conventional currency status.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Selling Live Animal for Meat of Same Species
Commentary on a disputed ribā ruling: meat for live animal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Ḥanafī position on selling a live animal for meat of the same species and evaluate the opposing view of Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
Selling a live animal for meat of its kind is permissible because meat is weighed (mawzūn) and live animals are not, thus no ribā.

The second ruling in the passage deals with exchanging a live animal for meat of the same species. The Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) permits it unconditionally, while Muhammad al-Shaybani requires the meat to be more than the meat in the animal to avoid ribā from non-meat parts. This lesson unpacks the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The transaction is selling a live animal for meat of the same species (e.g., a live sheep for lamb meat). The Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) permits it absolutely. Muhammad requires the meat to exceed the meat in the animal.
The dispute is about whether the live animal and meat are of the same category for ribā purposes.
2
Understand Muhammad's proof
Muhammad argues that if the meat is equal to or less than the meat in the animal, the non-meat parts (saqt) would be an excess, leading to ribā. Therefore, the meat must exceed to compensate for the saqt.
Saqt refers to parts like skin, spleen, intestines, etc., which are not meat.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue that the sale is between a weighed item (meat, mawzūn) and a non-weighed item (live animal, ghayr mawzūn). Since they are from different measurement categories, ribā does not apply.
Live animals are not sold by weight because they are difficult to weigh accurately.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If permissible absolutely, any amount of meat can be exchanged for a live animal. If Muhammad's condition applies, the meat must be more than the animal's meat, otherwise the sale is invalid.
The madhhab follows Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, so the condition is not required.

وجاز بيع الفلس بالفلسين بأعيانهما، واللحم بالحيوان
( وجاز بيع الفلس بالفلسين بأعيانهما : خلافاً لمحمد له ، له أنَّ الفلوس أثمان فلا تتعين بالتعيين فصار كما إذا كان بغير أعيانهما، وكبيع الدرهم بالدرهمين.
ولهما: أن ثمنيتها بالاصطلاح، واصطلاح الغير لا يكون حجة على المتعاقدين، وهما أبطلا ثمنيَّتها ؛ لأنهما قصدا تصحيح العقد، ولا وجه له إلا بتعينها وخروجها عن الثمنية، لأنها إذا خرجت من الثمنية تكون أعيانها مطلوبة لا ماليتها، فيمكن أن يُعطي فلسين ويأخذ فلساً طلبا لصورته. (واللحم بالحيوان) : خلافا لمحمد الله فإنَّ عنده إذا بيع الحيوان بلحم حيوان من جنسه " لا يجوز البيع إلا إذا كان اللحم أكثر من لحم ذلك الحيوان غير قبض ، جاز عندنا وعند الشافعي .
[١]قوله : له : إن...الخ ؛ حاصلهُ أنَّ الفلوس أثمان ثمنيّتها ثبتت باتفاقِ النَّاسِ كلّهم فلا تتعين بالتعين، ألا ترى أنه إذا اشترى ثوباً بفلوس معينة فهلكت قبل التسليم لا يبطل العقد، كما إذا اشترى بدراهم معينة أو دنانير معينة فهلكت.
ولما لم تتعين الفلوس فصار هذا البيع كما إذا باع فلساً بفلسين غير معين، أو درهماً بدرهمين، حيث لا يجوز البيعُ في هاتين الصورتين اتفاقاً ؛ لأنه يؤدّي إلى الربا فكذا ينبغي أن لا يجوز هاهنا.
[٢] قوله : لهما ... الخ ؛ حاصلُهُ : إِنّ الفلوس غير أثمان خلقة، بل الأصل فيها أن تكون مثمنة ؛ لكونها من النحاس، وهو غير ثمن ، والثمنية فيها إنما حصلت باصطلاح الناس، والعاقدان لم يصطلحا عليه، فتبطل ثمنيتُها قصداً لتصحيح العقد، إذ لا وجه للتصحيح إلا أن تكون معينة خارجة عن الثمنية، واصطلاح الغير لا يكون حجة عليهما، إذ لا ولاية للغير عليهما ، فلهما نقض ذلك الاصطلاح باصطلاح آخر. وإذا خرجت عن الثمنية فعادت مثمنة كما كانت ، وتكون أعيانها مطلوبة لا ماليتها ، فإنّ ماليتها لا تكون إلا باعتبار الثمنية وهي زائلة عنها باصطلاحهما، فيمكن أن يعطي فلسين ، ويأخذ فلساً واحداً طلباً لصورته لا باعتبار ماليته.
[٣]قوله : من جنسه ؛ وأما إذا كانا مختلفين، كما إذا باعَ لحم البقرِ بالشاةِ من مثلا

والدقيق بجنسه كيلاً، والرطب بالرطب وبالتّمر
زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية
[٢]
ليكون الزائد في مقابلة السَّقْطِ، وعندهما يجوز مطلقاً ؛ لأنه بيع الموزون بما ليس بموزون
( والدقيق بجنسه " كيلاً ، والرُّطب بالرُّطب وبالتّمر) : هذا عند أبي حنيفة له فيجوزُ بالاتفاق من غير اعتبار القلة والكثرة كما صرحوا به.
[١]قوله : ليكون... الخ ؛ يعني ليكون اللحم بمقابلة ما في الحيوان من اللحم ، والباقي بمقابلة السقط ، وهو غير اللحم كالجلد ونحوه ؛ لأنه لو لم يكن اللحم أكثر بل يكون مساوياً أو أقل.
فيثبت الربا من حيث زيادة السقط إذا كان اللحم مساوياً لما في الحيوان من اللحم أو من حيث زيادة اللحم إذا كان اللحم أكثر تما في الحيوان من اللحم، والسقط : بالتحريك، هيچ كاره از هر جيز. كذا في منتهى الأرب»، والمراد به هاهنا كل ما لا يطلق عليه اسم اللحم ؛ كالطحال والأمعاء، وغيرهما.
[٢) قوله : لأنه بيع ... الخ ؛ فإنَّ الحيوان لا يوزن عادة، ولا يمكن معرفة ثقله بالوزن؛ لأنه قد يخفِّفُ نفسه بصلابته ، فلا يدرى أنه خفِّفَ نفسه أو أثقل، وقد يثقل لاسترخاء مفاصله ، والميتُ لاسترخاء مفاصلهِ أثقل من الحي، والنساء لاسترخائهن أثقلُ من الرجال لصلابتهم ، كذا في «الكفاية)، وغيرها. وعند الشافعي له وأحمد الله : لا يجوز، ولا يجوز بالنَّسيئة إجماعاً. وفي «شرح الطحاوي»: لو كانت الشَّاةُ مذبوحة غير مسلوخة فاشتراها بلحم الشَّاةِ فالجواب في قولهم جميعاً كما قال محمد الله ، وأراد بغير المسلوخة غير المفصولة عن السقط، ولو اشترى شاة حيّة بشاة مذبوحة يجوز في قولهم جميعاً ، ذكره العلامة العيني له في «شرح الكنز».
[٣) قوله : والدقيق بجنسه ؛ متماثلاً كيلاً لا متفاضلاً لاتحاد الاسم والصورة والمعنى، وبه تثبت المجانسة من كل وجه، فلا يعتبر احتمال التفاضل كما في البرِّ بالبر. وقيده ابن الفضل لله بما إذا كانا مكبوسين، وإلا لا يجوز خلافاً للشافعي له ؛ لعدم
And it is permissible to sell a fals for two fulus by their specific selves, and meat for an animal. (And it is permissible to sell a fals for two fulus by their specific selves: contrary to Muhammad. He says that fulus are currencies, so they are not determined by specification, so it becomes like if it were not by their specific selves, and like selling a dirham for two dirhams. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): that their being currency is by convention, and the convention of others is not a proof against the two contractors, and they have nullified their being currency; because they intended to validate the contract, and there is no way except by specifying them and removing them from being currency, because when they leave being currency, their specific selves are sought, not their value, so he can give two fulus and take one fals seeking its form.) (And meat for an animal): contrary to Muhammad, for he holds that when an animal is sold for meat of an animal of its kind, the sale is not permissible unless the meat is more than the meat of that animal. Without possession, it is permissible according to us and according to al-Shafi'i. [1] His statement: 'He says...' etc.; its summary is that fulus are currencies; their being currency is established by the agreement of all people, so they are not determined by specification. Do you not see that if one buys a garment with specified fulus and they perish before delivery, the contract is not void, just as if one buys with specified dirhams or specified dinars and they perish. And since fulus are not determined, this sale becomes like selling a fals for two unspecified fulus, or a dirham for two dirhams, where the sale is not permissible in these two images unanimously; because it leads to riba. So likewise it should not be permissible here. [2] His statement: 'For them...' etc.; its summary is that fulus are not currencies by nature; rather, the original principle is that they are commodities because they are made of copper, which is not a currency. Their being currency only occurs by people's convention, and the two contractors have not agreed upon it, so they nullify their being currency intentionally to validate the contract. There is no way for validation except that they be specific and leave currency. The convention of others is not a proof against them, since others have no authority over them; so they have the right to revoke that convention with another convention. And when they leave currency, they return to being commodities as they were, and their specific selves are sought, not their value, for their value only exists by consideration of currency, and that is removed from them by their convention. So he can give two fulus and take one fals seeking its form, not considering its value. [3] His statement: 'Of its kind'; as for when they are different, like selling beef for a sheep, for example. And flour for its kind by measure, and fresh dates for fresh dates and for dried dates. Cream of the End for the Support of Care [2] So that the excess is in exchange for the saqt, and according to them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) it is permissible absolutely, because it is a sale of a weighed item for a non-weighed item. (And flour for its kind by measure, and fresh dates for fresh dates and for dried dates): this is according to Abu Hanifa, so it is permissible by agreement without considering scarcity or abundance as they have stated. [1] His statement: 'So that...' etc.; meaning so that the meat is in exchange for the meat in the animal, and the remainder is in exchange for the saqt, which is other than meat like skin and so on. Because if the meat is not more but equal or less, then riba would be established due to the increase of saqt if the meat equals the meat in the animal, or due to the increase of meat if the meat is more than the meat in the animal. And 'saqt' (with fath of the letter) is 'any thing' (in Persian). So in 'Muntaha al-Arab', and intended here is everything to which the name 'meat' is not applied, like spleen, intestines, and others. [2) His statement: 'Because it is a sale...' etc.; because the animal is not usually weighed, and its weight cannot be known by weighing; because it may lighten itself by its firmness, so it is not known whether it lightened itself or became heavier, and it may become heavy due to relaxation of its joints. The dead are heavier than the living due to relaxation of joints, and women are heavier than men due to their relaxation. So in 'al-Kifaya' and others. And according to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad, it is not permissible, and it is not permissible on credit by consensus. And in 'Sharh al-Tahawi': If the sheep was slaughtered but not skinned, and he bought it with meat of the sheep, the answer according to all of them is as Muhammad said, and he meant by 'not skinned' not separated from the saqt. And if one bought a live sheep for a slaughtered sheep, it is permissible according to all of them, mentioned by al-Allama al-Ayni in 'Sharh al-Kanz'. [3) His statement: 'And flour for its kind': similar by measure, not unequal, due to unity of name, form, and meaning, and thereby homogeneity is established from all aspects. So the possibility of inequality is not considered as in wheat for wheat. And Ibn al-Fadl restricted it to when they are pressed, otherwise it is not permissible contrary to al-Shafi'i; due to lack of...
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqaya. It discusses three distinct rulings within the chapter of ribā al-faḍl. The first ruling concerns exchanging one fals (copper coin) for two fulus by specifying the actual coins. The Hanafi position (Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf) permits it, while Muḥammad al-Shaybānī forbids it. The proof for Muḥammad is that fulus function as currency (thaman) by universal convention, so specifying them does not remove their currency status; thus the exchange must be equal, otherwise it leads to ribā. He analogizes to dirhams, which if specified and then perish before delivery do not void the contract, proving they remain currency. The Hanafi response is that fulus are not inherently currency; their thumaniyya is established by convention (iṣṭilāḥ). The two parties have the right to nullify that convention between themselves, making the coins mere commodities (aʿyān). By specifying them, they exit currency status, so exchanging two for one is permissible as a barter of specific items. The second ruling is selling a live animal for meat of the same species. Here Muḥammad requires the meat to be more than the meat contained in the live animal to account for non-meat parts (saqṭ). Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf allow it absolutely, arguing it is a sale of a weighed item (meat) for a non-weighed item (live animal), so no ribā. The commentary notes that animals are not usually weighed due to variation, reinforcing the difference in category. The third ruling covers flour for its kind by measure, and fresh dates for fresh dates or dried dates. According to Abū Ḥanīfa, these are permissible by agreement without consideration of equality, because they are homogeneous (mujānis) by name, form, and meaning when measured. The passage also mentions a condition by Ibn al-Faḍl that flour must be pressed, otherwise the ruling differs opposite al-Shāfiʿī.

Key Terms
سقط (saqt): The non-meat parts of an animal (skin, spleen, intestines, etc.) that are extraneous when exchanging meat for live animal.
موزون (mawzūn): An item sold by weight (weighed), such as meat. In ribā, mawzūn items are ribawī if exchanged with same kind.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Ḥanafī school (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) permit selling a live animal for meat of the same species without requiring the meat to exceed the animal's meat?
A) Because the meat is always more than the animal's meat.
B) Because the live animal is not sold by weight, so the sale is between a weighed and a non-weighed item.
C) Because the saqt is negligible.
D) Because Muhammad al-Shaybani later retracted his view.

Key Takeaway
The Ḥanafī madhhab permits selling a live animal for meat of the same species unconditionally, based on the distinction between weighed and non-weighed items.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
beginner
8 min
Selling Flour by Measure and Fresh Dates for Dried Dates
Commentary on a ruling from the matn: flour by measure and dates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Ḥanafī ruling on selling flour for flour by measure and fresh dates for fresh or dried dates, and identify the condition of homogeneity.

Core Idea
Homogeneous goods sold by measure are permissible even if unequal, as long as they are alike in name, form, and meaning.

The third ruling in the passage concerns selling flour for its kind by measure, and fresh dates for fresh dates or dried dates. According to Abu Hanifa, these sales are permissible by agreement without considering scarcity or abundance, provided the items are homogeneous (mujānis) in every respect. This lesson clarifies the ruling and a condition from later scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and ruling
What are the two transactions? (1) Selling flour for its kind (e.g., wheat flour for wheat flour) by measure (kayl), not by weight. (2) Selling fresh dates for fresh dates or dried dates. The ruling is permissible according to Abu Hanifa.
The ruling is from the matn and is agreed upon according to the commentary.
2
Understand the condition of homogeneity
For the sale to be valid, the items must be homogeneous (mujānis) by name, form, and meaning. This means they are of the same species and type. For example, flour from the same grain and dates of the same type.
If they are not homogeneous, different rulings apply.
3
Note the measurement method
The sale must be by measure (kayl), not by weight. This distinguishes it from other rulings where weight is required. The commentary mentions that Ibn al-Fadl restricted the permissibility to pressed flour; otherwise, it is not permissible.
The restriction is to ensure homogeneity; pressed flour ensures similarity in form.
4
Identify cross-madhhab difference
Al-Shafi'i does not permit flour for flour unless equal by weight. The Ḥanafī madhhab allows it by measure, considering that measure is the customary method for these items.
The difference stems from different criteria for ribawī items.

And it is permissible to sell a fals for two fulus by their specific selves, and meat for an animal. (And it is permissible to sell a fals for two fulus by their specific selves: contrary to Muhammad. He says that fulus are currencies, so they are not determined by specification, so it becomes like if it were not by their specific selves, and like selling a dirham for two dirhams. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): that their being currency is by convention, and the convention of others is not a proof against the two contractors, and they have nullified their being currency; because they intended to validate the contract, and there is no way except by specifying them and removing them from being currency, because when they leave being currency, their specific selves are sought, not their value, so he can give two fulus and take one fals seeking its form.) (And meat for an animal): contrary to Muhammad, for he holds that when an animal is sold for meat of an animal of its kind, the sale is not permissible unless the meat is more than the meat of that animal. Without possession, it is permissible according to us and according to al-Shafi'i. [1] His statement: 'He says...' etc.; its summary is that fulus are currencies; their being currency is established by the agreement of all people, so they are not determined by specification. Do you not see that if one buys a garment with specified fulus and they perish before delivery, the contract is not void, just as if one buys with specified dirhams or specified dinars and they perish. And since fulus are not determined, this sale becomes like selling a fals for two unspecified fulus, or a dirham for two dirhams, where the sale is not permissible in these two images unanimously; because it leads to riba. So likewise it should not be permissible here. [2] His statement: 'For them...' etc.; its summary is that fulus are not currencies by nature; rather, the original principle is that they are commodities because they are made of copper, which is not a currency. Their being currency only occurs by people's convention, and the two contractors have not agreed upon it, so they nullify their being currency intentionally to validate the contract. There is no way for validation except that they be specific and leave currency. The convention of others is not a proof against them, since others have no authority over them; so they have the right to revoke that convention with another convention. And when they leave currency, they return to being commodities as they were, and their specific selves are sought, not their value, for their value only exists by consideration of currency, and that is removed from them by their convention. So he can give two fulus and take one fals seeking its form, not considering its value. [3] His statement: 'Of its kind'; as for when they are different, like selling beef for a sheep, for example. And flour for its kind by measure, and fresh dates for fresh dates and for dried dates. Cream of the End for the Support of Care [2] So that the excess is in exchange for the saqt, and according to them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) it is permissible absolutely, because it is a sale of a weighed item for a non-weighed item. (And flour for its kind by measure, and fresh dates for fresh dates and for dried dates): this is according to Abu Hanifa, so it is permissible by agreement without considering scarcity or abundance as they have stated. [1] His statement: 'So that...' etc.; meaning so that the meat is in exchange for the meat in the animal, and the remainder is in exchange for the saqt, which is other than meat like skin and so on. Because if the meat is not more but equal or less, then riba would be established due to the increase of saqt if the meat equals the meat in the animal, or due to the increase of meat if the meat is more than the meat in the animal. And 'saqt' (with fath of the letter) is 'any thing' (in Persian). So in 'Muntaha al-Arab', and intended here is everything to which the name 'meat' is not applied, like spleen, intestines, and others. [2) His statement: 'Because it is a sale...' etc.; because the animal is not usually weighed, and its weight cannot be known by weighing; because it may lighten itself by its firmness, so it is not known whether it lightened itself or became heavier, and it may become heavy due to relaxation of its joints. The dead are heavier than the living due to relaxation of joints, and women are heavier than men due to their relaxation. So in 'al-Kifaya' and others. And according to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad, it is not permissible, and it is not permissible on credit by consensus. And in 'Sharh al-Tahawi': If the sheep was slaughtered but not skinned, and he bought it with meat of the sheep, the answer according to all of them is as Muhammad said, and he meant by 'not skinned' not separated from the saqt. And if one bought a live sheep for a slaughtered sheep, it is permissible according to all of them, mentioned by al-Allama al-Ayni in 'Sharh al-Kanz'. [3) His statement: 'And flour for its kind': similar by measure, not unequal, due to unity of name, form, and meaning, and thereby homogeneity is established from all aspects. So the possibility of inequality is not considered as in wheat for wheat. And Ibn al-Fadl restricted it to when they are pressed, otherwise it is not permissible contrary to al-Shafi'i; due to lack of...
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqaya. It discusses three distinct rulings within the chapter of ribā al-faḍl. The first ruling concerns exchanging one fals (copper coin) for two fulus by specifying the actual coins. The Hanafi position (Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf) permits it, while Muḥammad al-Shaybānī forbids it. The proof for Muḥammad is that fulus function as currency (thaman) by universal convention, so specifying them does not remove their currency status; thus the exchange must be equal, otherwise it leads to ribā. He analogizes to dirhams, which if specified and then perish before delivery do not void the contract, proving they remain currency. The Hanafi response is that fulus are not inherently currency; their thumaniyya is established by convention (iṣṭilāḥ). The two parties have the right to nullify that convention between themselves, making the coins mere commodities (aʿyān). By specifying them, they exit currency status, so exchanging two for one is permissible as a barter of specific items. The second ruling is selling a live animal for meat of the same species. Here Muḥammad requires the meat to be more than the meat contained in the live animal to account for non-meat parts (saqṭ). Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf allow it absolutely, arguing it is a sale of a weighed item (meat) for a non-weighed item (live animal), so no ribā. The commentary notes that animals are not usually weighed due to variation, reinforcing the difference in category. The third ruling covers flour for its kind by measure, and fresh dates for fresh dates or dried dates. According to Abū Ḥanīfa, these are permissible by agreement without consideration of equality, because they are homogeneous (mujānis) by name, form, and meaning when measured. The passage also mentions a condition by Ibn al-Faḍl that flour must be pressed, otherwise the ruling differs opposite al-Shāfiʿī.

Key Terms
جنس (jins): Kind, species; in ribā rulings, items of the same jins are subject to equality when exchanged.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because flour is not a ribawī item.
B) Because the items are homogeneous and sold by their customary measure.
C) Because the sale is on credit.
D) Because the quantity is fixed by weight.

Key Takeaway
The Ḥanafī school permits selling homogeneous goods like flour by measure without requiring equality, facilitating trade.

Exchanging Live Animal for Meat: Conditions and Ikhtilaf
Commentary on condition for permissible meat exchange

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on exchanging a live animal for meat of the same type, explain the condition required by Abu Hanifa, and distinguish the positions of Sahibayn and the Shafi'i/Hanbali schools.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa conditions excess meat to avoid riba; Sahibayn allow absolutely because a live animal is not weighed.

The exchange of a live animal for its meat raises questions of riba because both involve the same type of food. The Hanafi school has an internal difference: Abu Hanifa requires a condition to prevent riba, while his two disciples see no problem. Beyond the school, the Shafi'i and Hanbali schools prohibit the transaction entirely. In this lesson, you will unpack the reasoning behind each position and see how custom (urf) and analogy (qiyas) interact in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act under discussion is selling a live animal (e.g., a sheep) in exchange for meat of the same type (e.g., mutton). The ruling varies: Abu Hanifa says it is permissible only if the meat given exceeds the estimated meat of the animal; Sahibayn say it is absolutely permissible.
2
Locate the dalil for each position
Abu Hanifa's condition is based on qiyas to riba: if the meat were equal or less, the excess (of non-meat parts or meat) would constitute riba. Sahibayn rely on istihsan: live animals are not customarily weighed, so the transaction is like selling a weighed item for a non-weighed one, which is permissible.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
For Abu Hanifa, the excess meat compensates for non-meat parts (saqt) like skin and intestines. Without that excess, the exchange would involve a forbidden increase. For Sahibayn, the custom of not weighing live animals removes the analogy to riba.
Saqt is defined as everything not called meat.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Intra-Hanafi: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn. Cross-madhhab: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i/Hanbali (who prohibit the transaction outright).
It is also prohibited on credit by consensus.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah and the later tradition give preference to Abu Hanifa's position (mufta bihi), but Sahibayn's view is also recognised as valid.
The zahir al-riwayah is Abu Hanifa's conditional permissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[2] ليكون الزائد في مقابلة السَّقْطِ، وعندهما يجوز مطلقاً ؛ لأنه بيع الموزون بما ليس بموزون. [1] قوله : ليكون... الخ ؛ يعني ليكون اللحم بمقابلة ما في الحيوان من اللحم ، والباقي بمقابلة السقط ، وهو غير اللحم كالجلد ونحوه ؛ لأنه لو لم يكن اللحم أكثر بل يكون مساوياً أو أقل. فيثبت الربا من حيث زيادة السقط إذا كان اللحم مساوياً لما في الحيوان من اللحم أو من حيث زيادة اللحم إذا كان اللحم أكثر تما في الحيوان من اللحم، والسقط : بالتحريك، هيچ كاره از هر جيز. كذا في منتهى الأرب»، والمراد به هاهنا كل ما لا يطلق عليه اسم اللحم ؛ كالطحال والأمعاء، وغيرهما. [1] His statement: 'so that...' etc.; meaning that the meat is in exchange for the meat in the animal, and the remainder is in exchange for the non-meat parts, which is other than meat like skin and so forth; because if the meat is not more but equal or less, then riba is established due to the increase of non-meat parts if the meat equals that in the animal, or due to the increase of meat if the meat is more than that in the animal. Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling on exchanging meat from a live animal for meat of the same type. The commentator explains the condition set by Abu Hanifa for permissibility: the meat from the live animal must be more than the meat in the other to compensate for non-meat parts (saqt) like skin and intestines. If equal or less, riba occurs. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two companions) allow the exchange absolutely, reasoning that it is a sale of a weighed item (the meat) for a non-weighed item (the live animal), and since live animals are not customarily weighed, the prohibition of riba does not apply. The text also notes that Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad prohibit it, and it is forbidden on credit by consensus. The discussion highlights the Hanafi reliance on custom ('urf) and rational analogy (qiyas) in determining riba.

Key Terms

Key Takeaway
Exchanging live animal for its meat is permissible for Hanafis with conditions varying between Abu Hanifa and his companions, reflecting the role of custom and analogy in fiqh.

Selling Flour Like for Like by Volume: Hanafi Position and Dispute
Ruling on selling flour for flour by volume vs. weight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling flour of the same kind by volume and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding pressing (makkbusayn).

Core Idea
Flour of the same kind must be exchanged by volume, not weight, to avoid riba; Ibn al-Fadl requires pressing, but the relied-upon view does not.

This lesson unpacks a specific ruling from the matn of al-Quduri: selling flour of the same kind must be by volume and equal. The commentator expands on the rationale, introduces an intra-Hanafi condition from Ibn al-Fadl, and contrasts with the Shafi'i position. Understanding this helps you see how the Hanafi school ensures riba-free exchange in homogeneous goods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Basis
The matn states: flour of its kind must be exchanged by volume (kaylan) and equal (mimathlayn). The underlying reason is mujanasah (homogeneity) due to unity of name, form, and meaning.
This is analogous to wheat-for-wheat exchange.
2
Understand the Condition of Volume (Kaylan)
The phrase 'kaylan' excludes weight (because there are two narrations on weight) and excludes lump sum (juzaf). Volume ensures exact equivalence to prevent riba.
Weight is not a reliable measure for these items due to possible differences in density.
3
Analyze Ibn al-Fadl's Restriction
Ibn al-Fadl adds a condition: both flours must be pressed (makkbusayn) so that the measure is filled without gaps. Otherwise, he deems the exchange invalid due to lack of moderation in the measure.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the matn does not mention pressing.
4
Note the Cross-Madhhab Difference
Imam al-Shafi'i allows the exchange even without pressing, as he does not require moderation in a single measurement. The Hanafi commentator mentions this to highlight the rigour of Ibn al-Fadl's condition.
The Shafi'i position is more lenient.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa), selling flour by volume without pressing is permissible as long as the volumes are equal. The two narrations on weight indicate that weight is not a valid substitute.
Mufta bihi: Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[٣) قوله : والدقيق بجنسه ؛ متماثلاً كيلاً لا متفاضلاً لاتحاد الاسم والصورة والمعنى، وبه تثبت المجانسة من كل وجه، فلا يعتبر احتمال التفاضل كما في البرِّ بالبر. وقيده ابن الفضل لله بما إذا كانا مكبوسين، وإلا لا يجوز خلافاً للشافعي له ؛ لعدم الاعتدال في دخوله الكيل ؛ لأنه منكبس وممتلئ جداً ، وقوله : كيلاً ؛ احتراز عن الوزن؛ لأنَّ فيه روايتين، وعن الجزاف وإشارة إلى نفي قوله : الشافعي له، كذا «مجمع الأنهر» ("
And flour with its kind by volume, and fresh dates with fresh dates and with dry dates: this is according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) so it is permissible by agreement without regard to quantity as they stated. [3] His statement: 'And flour with its kind' meaning equal by volume, not by excess, because of unity of name, form, and meaning, and thereby homogeneity is established in every respect, so possibility of excess is not considered as in wheat for wheat. And Ibn al-Fadl (may Allah have mercy on him) restricted it to when both are pressed (makkbusayn), otherwise it is not permissible, contrary to al-Shafi'i, because of lack of moderation in entering the measure; because it is pressed and very full. Thus in Majma' al-Anhur.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi ruling on selling flour of the same kind for flour. The matn states it must be by volume (kaylan) and equal. According to Abu Hanifa, it is permissible by agreement without regard to quantity, as long as it is by volume. The commentator notes that the condition of equality by volume is due to the unity of name, form, and meaning, establishing full homogeneity (mujanasah) so that potential excess is not considered, similar to wheat-for-wheat exchange. However, Ibn al-Fadl restricts this to when both are pressed (makkbusayn), otherwise it is not permissible - contrary to Shafi'i who allows it even without pressing. The phrase 'by volume' excludes weight (because there are two narrations about weight), and excludes lump sum (juzaf). This highlights the Hanafi emphasis on precise measurement in ribawi transactions.

Key Terms
كيلاً (kaylan): Measurement by volume, e.g., using a litre container.
مكبوسين (makkbusayn): Pressed or compacted so that the measure is filled without air gaps.
متماثلاً (mutamathilan): Equal, identical in measure.
جزاف (juzaf): Lump sum estimation without precise measurement.
مجانسة (mujanasah): Full homogeneity in genus, form, and meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Flour of the same kind must be exchanged by volume, not weight, to prevent riba; pressing is an additional condition from Ibn al-Fadl but not required in the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Selling Fresh Dates for Dry Dates: The 'Illah of Decrease upon Drying
Proof from hadith for prohibition of selling fresh dates for dry dates

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling fresh dates for dry dates, identify the hadith proof, and explain the 'illah (cause) of decrease upon drying that renders the sale impermissible.

Core Idea
Selling fresh dates for dry dates is prohibited because fresh dates shrink upon drying, causing unknown inequality.

In this lesson, we examine a classical riba-related sale: exchanging fresh dates (rutab) for dried dates (tamr). The Hanafi school, following the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), rules this impermissible if the fresh dates decrease when dried. The primary proof is a hadith from the Muwatta' of Imam Malik where the Prophet (PBUH) forbids it after confirming the decrease. Understanding the 'illah (cause) of shrinkage is key to applying the rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its proponents
State the Hanafi position and note that Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafiʿi prohibit the sale if fresh dates decrease upon drying. Also note the reported view of Abu Hanifa who may have allowed it.
The text says 'according to them (the two companions) and al-Shafiʿi, it is not permissible if the fresh decreases.'
2
Locate the hadith proof
Find the hadith of Saʿd ibn Abi Waqqas in the Muwatta': the Prophet asked if fresh dates decrease when dried, and upon affirmative answer, he forbade the transaction.
The hadith is a mursal from Zayd Abu ʿAyyash. Al-Hidayah remarks on its chain.
3
Understand the 'illah: decrease upon drying
The prohibition is based on the certain loss of volume upon drying, which leads to inequality in the exchange of a ribawi commodity (dates).
The text explicitly identifies 'the decrease upon drying' as the 'illah.
4
Note the condition for permissibility: equivalence after drying
The condition for a valid sale is equivalence in the most balanced state, which is after drying. Since this is unknown at the time of sale, the transaction is corrupt.
A qafiz of fresh dates may become half a qafiz when dry.
5
Compare with similar cases
The text analogizes to selling flour for wheat, which is also prohibited due to disparity after grinding. This illustrates the broader principle of riba in weight-based commodities.
Both cases involve an unknown and inevitable change in quantity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And as for according to them (the two companions) and according to al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him), it is not permissible if the fresh dates decrease upon drying. They said: Yes. So he forbade it.' End. And Muhammad said in al-Muwatta': 'We take this; there is no good in a man buying a qafiz of fresh dates for a qafiz of dry dates hand to hand, because the fresh decreases when it dries and becomes less than a qafiz; therefore the sale is corrupt.' End. So in this hadith there is an indication of the cause ('illah), which is the decrease upon drying, and by that it is made clear that the condition for permissibility is equivalence in the most balanced state, which is after drying, and that is not known by equality in volume at the moment because a qafiz of fresh dates may become half a qafiz when dry. Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on selling fresh dates for dry dates, a classic riba issue. According to Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i, the sale is not permissible if the fresh dates decrease upon drying. The commentator provides the evidence from the Muwatta' of Imam Malik: a hadith where the Prophet (PBUH) forbade buying fresh dates for dry dates because fresh dates shrink when dried. Imam Muhammad confirms this position, stating that the sale is corrupt because the fresh dates become less after drying. The underlying cause ('illah) is the inevitable loss of volume upon drying, so equivalence must be measured at the state after drying, which is unknown at the time of sale. This is analogous to selling flour for wheat, which is also prohibited due to disparity after grinding. The passage also notes a critical remark from al-Hidayah about the reliability of the hadith chain (Zayd ibn 'Ayyash).

Key Terms
الرطب (al-rutab): Fresh dates (moist, not dried)
التمر (al-tamr): Dried dates
العلة (al-'illah): The effective cause (decrease upon drying) that triggers the prohibition

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the underlying cause ('illah) for prohibiting fresh dates for dry dates according to the hadith?
A) Difference in taste
B) Decrease in volume upon drying
C) Prohibition of credit sales
D) Lack of homogeneity

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of selling fresh dates for dry dates is based on the certain decrease upon drying, making equivalence unknown; this hadith-based ruling is mufta bihi in the Hanafi school.

Selling Fresh Dates for Dried Dates: Hanafi Position and Dispute
Inter-madhhab dispute with proofs and narrator criticism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on selling fresh dates for dried dates and evaluate the opposing evidence.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa permits fresh-for-dried date sale if equal by volume at contract; shrinkage is irrelevant.

This lesson dives into a classic inter-madhhab dispute: can you sell a qafiz of fresh dates for a qafiz of dried dates hand to hand? The Hanafi school, led by Abu Hanifa, says yes if they are equal in volume at the time of contract. But the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), along with al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad, say no, because the fresh dates will shrink when dried. The debate turns on which hadith takes precedence and the reliability of a key narrator. By the end, you will understand not only the ruling but also how Hanafis navigate contradictory proofs.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa: the sale is permissible if the fresh and dried dates are equal by volume at the time of contract. Note the condition of equality (mumathala) and the time at which it is measured.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opposing position and its proof
The Sahibayn, al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad hold that the sale is invalid if the fresh dates shrink. Their evidence is the hadith of Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas narrated by Zayd Abu Ayyash, where the Prophet forbade buying fresh dates for dried dates because of shrinkage.
The opposing proof is based on an analogy: the shrinkage makes the exchange unequal after drying.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the opposing proof
Abu Hanifa responds that the prohibition in that hadith is due to uncertainty of equality, not the exchange itself. Instead, he relies on the general hadith of Ubadah ibn al-Samit which permits dates-for-dates if equal, and allows differing types freely. The Hanafi view prioritizes the general over the specific.
The Hanafi response also questions the reliability of Zayd Abu Ayyash, but many later scholars defend his trustworthiness.
4
Evaluate the narrator criticism and its impact
The author of al-Hidayah claimed Zayd is weak, but al-'Ayni and others say he is trustworthy. This affects the strength of the opposing proof. Assess whether the criticism of the narrator is valid or not, and how it influences the school's reliance on Abu Hanifa's opinion.
The debate over narrator reliability does not change the mufta bihi position, but it strengthens the Hanafi argument.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference in rulings leads to a practical outcome: under Abu Hanifa, such sales are valid and enforceable; under the opponents, they are fasid (corrupt) and must be rescinded. This affects everyday transactions in date markets.
Knowing the thamarah helps a mufti advise the public correctly.

Source Text

(۲) وروي عن مالك في «الموطأ»، وهو لا يروي عن مجهول، وقال المنذري: كيف يكون مجهولاً وقد روى عنه ثقتان: عبد الله بن يزيد، وعمران بن أبي أنيس، وهما مما احتج به مسلم في صحيحه»، وقد عرفه أئمة هذا الشأن، وأخرج حديثه مالك مع شدة تحريه في الرجال. وقال ابن الجوزي في «التحقيق»: قال أبو حنيفة: إنه مجهول، فإن كان هو لم يعرفه فقد عرفه أئمة النقل. انتهى. هذا وإن شئت زيادة التحقيق فارجع إلى «تهذيب التهذيب» للمحقق العَسْقَلاني، و«تخريج الهداية» للعلامة الزَّيْلَعِي، و«شرح معاني الآثار» للمحقق الطحاوي; لئلا تلتبس عليك الحال، ويظهر لك ما قالوا من توجيهات المقال. ووجه قول أبي حنيفة: قوله في حديث عبادة بن الصامت: «والتمر بالتمر بمثل، والملح بالملح مثلاً بمثل، سواء بسواء، يداً بيد، فإذا اختلفت هذه الأصناف فبيعوا كيف شئتم»
And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and according to al-Shafi'i, it is not permissible if the fresh dates decrease upon drying. Moderation in its entry into measurement [by volume] is because it is compressed and very full, and his statement: 'by measure' is a precaution against weight because there are two narrations concerning it, and against estimation, and an indication to negate the statement of al-Shafi'i. Likewise in Majma' al-Anhur. [1] His statement: 'It is not permissible if it decreases...' etc.; and it is the statement of Malik and Ahmad, and their evidence is what Malik narrated in al-Muwatta': from Abdullah ibn Yazid, the freed slave of al-Aswad ibn Sufyan: 'That Zayd Abu Ayyash, the freed slave of Banu Zuhra, informed him that he asked Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas about one who bought al-Bayda' meaning barley with al-Sult – meaning a type of barley that has no husk in the Hijaz, as al-Jawhari stated. Sa'd said to him: Which is better? He said: al-Bayda'. He said: He forbade me from it, and said: I heard the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, being asked about one who buys dates with fresh dates. He said: Does the fresh date decrease when it dries? They said: Yes. He forbade it. End. And Muhammad said in al-Muwatta': 'We take this. There is no good in a man buying a qafiz of fresh dates for a qafiz of dried dates hand to hand, because the fresh dates decrease when they dry and become less than a qafiz; therefore the sale is corrupt.' End. So in this hadith there is an indication of the cause ('illa), which is the decrease at the time of drying, and by it it becomes clear that the condition for permissibility is equality in the most balanced state, which is after drying, and that is not known by equality in measure at the moment, because a qafiz of fresh dates might become half a qafiz when dry, and that is like selling flour for wheat, which is not permissible due to inequality after grinding. And know that the author of al-Hidayah said: The basis of what they narrated is on Zayd ibn Ayyash, and he is weak according to the transmitters. End. But Allamah al-'Ayni said in al-Binaya: This is not correct; rather he is trustworthy according to the transmitters. End. And he said in Ghayat al-Bayan Sharh al-Hidayah: They transmitted his weakening from Abu Hanifa, but his weakness is not established in the books of hadith, and whoever claims it must provide evidence. End. And he said in Fath al-Qadir: The author of al-Tanqih said: Zayd ibn Ayyash Abu Ayyash al-Zuraqi al-Madani, there is no problem with him, and our scholars mentioned from Abu Hanifa that he is unknown, and his criticism is rejected because he is trustworthy. (2) And it is narrated from Malik in al-Muwatta', and he does not narrate from an unknown person. And al-Mundhiri said: How can he be unknown when two trustworthy people narrated from him: Abdullah ibn Yazid and Imran ibn Abi Anis, both of whom Muslim used as evidence in his Sahih. And the imams of this field knew him, and Malik narrated his hadith despite his strictness in [scrutinizing] narrators. And Ibn al-Jawzi said in al-Tahqiq: Abu Hanifa said he is unknown; if he (Abu Hanifa) did not know him, the imams of transmission knew him. End. And if you want further verification, refer to Tahdhib al-Tahdhib by the researcher al-'Asqalani, and Takhrij al-Hidayah by Allamah al-Zayla'i, and Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar by the researcher al-Tahawi, so that the situation is not confused for you, and the directions of the discourse become clear to you. And the basis of Abu Hanifa's statement: his statement in the hadith of Ubadah ibn al-Samit: 'Dates for dates equal for equal, salt for salt equal for equal, like for like, hand to hand; and if these types differ, then sell as you wish.'
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the sale of fresh dates (rutab) for dried dates (tamr). The Hanafi school's position is presented alongside the views of Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, Imam al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad. The core issue is whether such an exchange is permissible when the fresh dates are measured by volume at the time of sale, knowing they will shrink upon drying.

According to Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i, the sale is not permissible if the fresh dates decrease when dried. Their evidence is a hadith narrated by Malik in al-Muwatta' via Zayd Abu Ayyash, who asked Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas about buying barley with a different type of barley. Sa'd forbade it, analogizing to the Prophet's prohibition of buying fresh dates for dried dates because the fresh ones shrink. Muhammad al-Shaybani endorses this view in his version of al-Muwatta'.

However, Abu Hanifa holds that the sale is permissible if the fresh dates are equal in measure at the time of sale, because the condition for permissibility is equality in the most balanced state, which is after drying. He argues that the prohibition in the hadith is due to the uncertainty of equality, not the mere fact of exchanging fresh for dried. Abu Hanifa relies on the hadith of Ubadah ibn al-Samit, which explicitly permits selling dates for dates equal for equal, and allows differing kinds to be sold freely.

The text then addresses a critical issue: the reliability of Zayd ibn Ayyash, the narrator of the hadith used by the opponents. The author of al-Hidayah claimed he is weak, but later scholars like al-'Ayni, the author of Ghayat al-Bayan, and others defended his trustworthiness. The passage concludes by directing those who seek further verification to major works of hadith criticism.

The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of this dispute is that according to Abu Hanifa, one may buy a qafiz of fresh dates for a qafiz of dried dates hand to hand, provided they are equal in volume at the time of contract. According to the Sahibayn and al-Shafi'i, such a transaction is invalid (fasid) because the fresh dates will shrink, making the exchange unequal.

Key Terms
الرطب (al-rutab): Fresh dates that are moist and have a high water content.
التمر (al-tamr): Dried dates that have lost moisture through drying.
المماثلة (al-mumathala): Equality in measure or weight, which is a condition in certain sales to avoid riba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa), what is the condition for the validity of selling fresh dates (rutab) for dried dates (tamr) by volume?
A) They must be equal in weight at the time of contract.
B) They must be equal in volume at the time of contract.
C) The fresh dates must not shrink when dried.
D) The sale must be deferred until the fresh dates are dried.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa permits selling fresh dates for dried dates if they are equal by volume at the time of contract; the shrinkage upon drying does not invalidate the sale.

Defending the Narrator and Ruling on Rutab bi Tamr
Biographical defense and ruling derivation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to defend the reliability of Zayd ibn Ayyash using scholarly citations and apply the hadith of Ubada ibn al-Samit to state the Hanafi ruling on selling fresh dates for dried dates.

Core Idea
A hadith's authenticity and ruling are both defended by scholarly consensus on the narrator's reliability.

This passage addresses two linked issues: the reliability of the narrator Zayd ibn Ayyash and the fiqh ruling derived from his hadith. Abu Hanifa reportedly considered him unknown, but later hadith masters affirmed his trustworthiness, thereby validating the proof. The ruling on selling fresh dates for dried dates is then derived from the hadith of Ubada ibn al-Samit, which distinguishes between same-genus and different-genus exchanges. Understanding this defense strengthens your ability to evaluate both riwaya and diraya in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the narrator and the challenge
Zayd ibn Ayyash is the narrator of the hadith on date exchange. Abu Hanifa reportedly declared him unknown (majhul). Note the source of this claim.
The text quotes al-Tanqih and Ibn al-Jawzi that Abu Hanifa's statement was transmitted but not definitively proven.
2
Evaluate the counter-arguments
Scholars like al-Ayni, al-Mundhiri, and Ibn al-Jawzi defend Zayd: he is declared reliable, narrated from by two trustworthy narrators used by Muslim, and Malik narrated from him despite his strictness.
The principle: if multiple reliable scholars affirm a narrator, a single doubt does not render the hadith weak.
3
Extract the hadith and its legal structure
The hadith of Ubada ibn al-Samit: 'Dates for dates, like for like, salt for salt, like for like, equal for equal, hand to hand; if these types differ, then sell as you wish.'
The hadith establishes a general rule for ribawi items: same genus requires equality and hand-to-hand; different genera allow free exchange.
4
Derive the ruling for rutab bi tamr
The commentator argues: if rutab is considered the same genus as tamr (dates), the first part applies (equality + hand-to-hand); if different, the second part applies (free exchange). The difference in moisture is like quality difference and does not affect genus.
Abu Hanifa's position: rutab and tamr are of the same genus, so sale must be equal by weight and immediate.

And the aspect of inference: If fresh dates are dates, then the sale is permitted by the first part of the hadith, i.e., his saying 'dates for dates'; and if they are not dates, then by the later part of the hadith, i.e., his saying 'if these types differ, then sell as you wish'. Also, the difference between fresh and dried dates is by original creation, so it is disregarded like the difference between good and bad. And you should refer to 'Fath al-Qadir' if you wish to verify this topic, for it contains what cures the ill.

Commentary
This passage begins with a biographical defense of the narrator Zayd ibn Ayyash, whose reliability was questioned by Abu Hanifa. The commentator cites multiple scholars (al-Ayni, al-Mundhiri, Ibn al-Jawzi) affirming his trustworthiness. The purpose is to validate the hadith used as proof. The ruling is on selling fresh dates (rutab) for dried dates (tamr). The hadith from Ubada ibn al-Samit establishes two principles: if two items are of the same genus (e.g., dates), they must be equal in measure and exchanged hand-to-hand; if they are of different genera (e.g., dates and salt), they may be sold freely. The commentator argues that fresh dates are either part of the date genus (if considered the same as dried dates) or a different genus; in either case, the sale is permissible with equality if same genus, or without equality if different. The difference between rutab and tamr is attributed to original creation, like the difference between high and low quality, which does not affect the permissibility of exchange in different genera. This position is specific to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
الرطب (al-rutab): Fresh dates, as opposed to dried dates (tamr).
التمر (al-tamr): Dried dates.
مجهول (majhul): An unknown narrator whose identity or reliability is not established.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The hadith is widely known and practiced by the community.
B) Multiple hadith scholars confirmed his reliability and Malik narrated from him.
C) Abu Hanifa later retracted his statement.
D) The hadith is also reported through other chains.

Key Takeaway
The reliability of a narrator is established by the consensus of hadith critics, and once the hadith is authenticated, the ruling derived from it (equality for same-genus ribawi items) is binding in the Hanafi school.

Selling Grapes for Raisins: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on selling grapes for raisins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on selling grapes for raisins and explain the reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
The Sahibayn require sale items to be in their most balanced state; grape and raisin moisture differs, making equality unverifiable.

In this passage, the commentator explains an intra-Hanafi dispute over whether grapes may be sold for raisins. The ruling depends on whether they are considered the same genus and on the condition of the items at the time of contract. Abu Hanifa permits with equality in measure; the Sahibayn prohibit outright.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and disputed ruling
Recognize that the act is a sale of grapes for raisins. Abu Hanifa holds it permissible if measures are equal; the Sahibayn hold it impermissible regardless of equality.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi, not between schools.
2
Trace the reasoning (ta'lil)
Abu Hanifa analogizes to the sale of fresh dates for dried dates: if same genus, equality in measure suffices. The Sahibayn require similarity in the most balanced state (a'dal al-ahwal), which cannot be verified due to moisture difference.
The core difference: what condition is required for permissibility.
3
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, the sale is valid if equal measure; under the Sahibayn, it is invalid. This affects real transactions.
The mufta bihi is not specified here.
4
Note the intra-madhhab status
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school. No explicit tarjih is given in this passage.
Later authorities may adopt one view based on additional considerations.

قوله : والعنب بالزبيب ; على الخلاف المذكور، فيجوز البيع عند أبي حنيفة إذا تساويا كيلاً، وعندهما : لا يجوز سواءً تساويا أو تفاضلا ، والوجه ما تقدم في بيع الرطب بالتمر وهو على قوله : إنَّ الزبيب مع العنب إن كان جنساً واحداً جاز بيعُ أحدهما بالآخر كيلاً متماثلاً. وإن كانا جنسين فجازَ أيضاً لاختلاف الصنّف ، وأما على قولهما فشرط الجواز هو المماثلة في أعدل الأحوال، وذا لا يعرفُ بالمساواة كيلاً في الحال ، وقيل : لا يجوز بالاتفاق كما في الحنطة المقلية بغير المقلية.
His saying: 'And grapes for raisins' — based on the mentioned difference: selling is permissible according to Abu Hanifa if they are equal in measure; according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is not permissible whether they are equal or unequal. The reasoning is as previously in the sale of fresh dates for dried dates. According to his (Abu Hanifa's) view, if raisins and grapes are one genus, then selling one for the other is permissible with equality in measure; if they are two genera, then it is also permissible due to difference in type. According to the two, the condition for permissibility is similarity in the most balanced state, and that is not known by equality in measure at the present time. And it is said: It is not permissible by consensus, as in fried wheat versus non-fried.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on selling grapes for raisins. Abu Hanifa applies the same reasoning as for fresh and dried dates: if they are the same genus, equality in measure is required; if different genera, no equality needed. Based on this, since both are fruits of the vine, they may be considered one genus, so equality in measure suffices for permissibility. The two disciples (Sahibayn) require the items to be in the most balanced state at the time of contract, which cannot be verified by mere equality in measure because the moisture content differs; hence they prohibit the sale entirely. A minority view says it is unanimously prohibited, like fried wheat for non-fried.

Key Terms
العنب (al-inab): Fresh grapes.
الزبيب (al-zabib): Raisins (dried grapes).
التماثل في أعدل الأحوال (al-tamathul fi a'dal al-ahwal): Equality in the most balanced state (i.e., when both items are in a neutral, stable condition).

C) Because the hadith explicitly prohibits it.
D) Because Abu Hanifa's opinion is followed by consensus.

Key Takeaway
Selling grapes for raisins is permissible per Abu Hanifa with equality, but the Sahibayn prohibit it due to the difficulty of verifying true equality when moisture content differs.

Selling Wet Barley for Dry Barley and Defining Munqa'
Ruling on moist barley sale and lexical note on munqa'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling moist barley for dry barley and identify the condition that makes it permissible or impermissible according to Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
Selling moist grain for dry grain is permissible only if moisture has not caused swelling; otherwise it is impermissible due to riba.

The sale of foodstuffs of the same kind must be equal in measure and free from deferred riba. When one batch is wet and the other dry, equality is uncertain. This passage clarifies Muhammad al-Shaybani's position on selling moist barley for dry barley, including a condition based on swelling, and then defines the term 'al-munqa'' (soaked raisins).

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputing positions
Note that Muhammad prohibits the sale if swelling occurs, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad? Actually 'huma' refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad? The text says 'huma ya'tabirani hal al-aqd' but then mentions Abu Yusuf's exception for dates. Recognize the intra-Hanafi difference: Muhammad's condition and Abu Yusuf's hadith-based exception.
The pronoun 'huma' likely refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in the context of the condition of the contract, but the exception is specific to Abu Yusuf.
2
Locate the condition for permissibility
From Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani's clarification: if the wheat has been wetted but not swollen, it is permissible to sell it for dry wheat if equal in measure.
The swelling is key; recent wetting that does not alter the grain's integrity is allowed.
3
Understand the lexical note
Examine the definition of 'al-munqa'': soaked raisins, from the verb anqa'a, meaning to soak in a vat to extract sweetness. Note its rare usage in language books.
The term appears in the context of riba rulings for raisins.
4

Consider a sale of 10 kg recently wetted barley (not swollen) for 10 kg dry barley. According to the narration from Muhammad, this is permissible if equal in measure.

Exchanging Grapes for Raisins: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Riba
Commentary on riba rulings for grapes and raisins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on exchanging grapes for raisins and explain why Abu Hanifa permits it while the Sahibayn prohibit it.

Core Idea
Grapes and raisins: one genus or two? The dispute turns on whether moisture negates equality.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the exchange of grapes for raisins. While Abu Hanifa permits the transaction if the measures are equal, his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, forbid it entirely. To understand the reasoning, we must delve into the classification of genera and the role of moisture in riba rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Disputed Ruling
What exactly is being exchanged? Grapes for raisins. The ruling is contested: Abu Hanifa says permissible if equal in measure; the Sahibayn say not permissible at all.
Note that this is a sale transaction subject to riba rules.
2
State the Two Positions Clearly
Abu Hanifa: permissible with equality in measure (kayl). Sahibayn: impermissible whether equal or not.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Dalil: Genus Classification
Abu Hanifa argues that grapes and raisins are one genus (jins wahid) because they share the same origin. The hadith 'dates for dates' and 'if types differ, sell as you wish' support this. Equality in measure is sufficient.
Moisture difference is considered negligible, like quality differences.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's Dalil: Condition of Equality
The Sahibayn require equality in the 'most balanced state' (a'dal al-ahwal). Since grapes are wet and raisins dry, their true equivalence is unknown at the time of contract, so the condition is not met.
They prioritize certainty in avoiding riba.
5
Determine Thamarah al-Khilaf and Mufta Bihi
Practical consequence: Abu Hanifa's view allows such exchanges with immediate measurement; Sahibayn's view prohibits them entirely. The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's, as adopted by later Hanafi authorities.
This is the operative fatwa for everyday transactions.
